Objection and Reply on Potentiality vs. Actuality
Objection and reply on dual capacities of substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a substance's two capacities (reception of accidents and spatial occupation) require two causes, and the reply that the discussion is about potentiality, not actuality, so only one capacity is involved.

Core Idea
Potentiality for multiple effects is one attribute, not many.

The commentator begins by presenting an objection: a substance seems to have two distinct effects—receiving accidents and occupying space—which would suggest two causes. However, he replies that the discussion concerns potentiality, not actuality, and the potential for both is a single capacity. This lesson explains that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection claims that the substance's two effects arise from two different conditions (the state within it and the space around it).
2
Identify the reply's key move
The commentator shifts the focus from actual reception to potentiality. He asserts that potentiality for both is one attribute of the essence.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does it matter that the potentiality is one? How does this defend the possibility of multiple effects from a single cause?
This connects to the broader discourse on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : استناد آثار متعددة إلى مؤثر واحد حاصل حقيقي. لا يقال: أحدهما وهو قبول الأعراض أثر للجوهر (باعتبار الحال) فيه، وهو العرض، والآخر وهو التحيز أثر له (باعتبار الحيز الذي يتمكن فيه)، فقد تعدد هاهنا الشرط. لأنا نقول: ليس كلامنا في كونه محلاً للعرض بالفعل، وكونه في الحيز بالفعل حتى يكون صدورهما عنه بتوسط الحال والحيز، كما ذكرتم بل الكلام في قابليته لهما، وهو أي كونه قابلا لهما من عوارض ذاته المعللة بها. والحق أنه لا يتم هذا الاستدلال إلا ببيان بساطة العلة التي هي الجوهرية، ولا يمكن أخذه إلزامياً لأن الجوهر عندهم خمسة أقسام، والقابل منها ابتداء، إذ بعد ثبوت هذا القول لا حاجة لنا إلى إثبات ذلك الجواز، فلا يرد أن هذا الاستدلال لا أما إلزامياً فلما ذكره الشارح قدس سره، وأما تحقيقياً، فلعدم قولهم بالعلية فيما يكد يصح، سوى ذاته تعالى. قوله : (بل الكلام في قابليته لهما ) فيه أنه على هذا التقدير يكون مصدراً لأثر واحد، وهو القابلية إلا أن يثبت تخلف القابليتين بالماهية. قوله : ( من عوارض ذاته إلخ من غير توسط الحال ،والحيز وإن كان الحكم بثبوتها له بتوسط تعلقهما. العارضة بينهما، فمراد ذلك المتوهم أن الموجب إذا كان واحداً حقيقياً قبل الإيجاب لا يمكن أن يصدر عنه بالإيجاب أكثر من واحد ، وأما إذا كان المختار واحداً حقيقياً قبل الاختيار فيجوز أن يصدر عنه بالاختيار آثار متعددة، وهذا كلام لا غبار عليه فليتأمل قوله : ( لنا في إثبات الجواز الجوهرية إلخ) قيل عليه : لما كانت الحوادث مستندة إلى الله تعالى بلا واسطة عند الأشاعرة، لم يصح لهم الاستدلال بالجوهرية على جواز صدور المعلولين عن الواحد الحقيقي، فلا وجه في إثبات المدعى بمجرد البناء على الإلزام. قوله : (إلا) ببيان بساطة العلة التي هي (الجوهرية مع أنها ليست بسيطة، فإن لها وجوداً وماهية وإمكاناً وجنساً وفصلاً، وغير ذلك. فإن قلت : هي بجميع ما فيها ولها شيء واحد مستند إليه، كل من الأمرين ولا معنى لاستناد الكثير إلى الواحد سوى هذا والحاصل : أن المناقشة إنما ترد إذا استند أحد الأمرين إليها باعتبار بعض جهاتها، والآخر باعتبار جهتها الأخرى، وهاهنا ليس كذلك قلت لا نسلم أنه ليس كذلك فإن الوجود أشرف من الإمكان، وقبول الأعراض لكون متبوعه أشرف من التحيز الذي يفيد الاحتياج إلى الحيز فجاز أن يستند الأشرف إلى الأشرف، والأخس إلى الأخس كما علم من قاعدتهم في بيان كيفية صدور الممكنات عن الواجب.
The Fifth Observatory - Third Goal: The attribution of multiple effects to one efficient cause. A real result. It is not said: One of them, which is the reception of accidents, is an effect of the substance (by virtue of the state) in it, and it is the accident; and the other, which is spatial occupation, is an effect of it (by virtue of the space in which it is located); so the condition has become multiple here. For we say: Our speech is not about its being a locus for the accident actually, and its being in the space actually, such that their emanation from it would be mediated by the state and the space, as you mentioned; rather, speech is about its potentiality for them, i.e., its being potential for them, which is among the accidents of its essence that are caused by it. And the truth is that this argument is not completed except by demonstrating the simplicity of the cause, which is substance-hood, and it cannot be taken as obligatory because substance according to them has five divisions, and the potential among them is primary; for after establishing this statement, we have no need to prove that possibility. So it is not objected that this argument is not obligatory due to what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned, nor is it demonstrative because they do not affirm causality except with respect to His essence, may He be exalted. His statement: 'Rather, speech is about its potentiality for them' — in it, that according to this estimation, it would be the source of a single effect, namely potentiality, unless the two potentialities are proven to differ in quiddity. His statement: 'from the accidents of its essence, etc., without mediation of the state and space even if the judgment of their existence for it is by virtue of their relation to them.' The accident between them; so the intention of that one who imagines is that if the necessitator is truly one before necessitation, it is impossible for more than one to proceed from it by necessitation. But if the chooser is truly one before choice, it is permissible for multiple effects to proceed from him by choice. This is speech with no dust on it, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'for us in proving the possibility of substance-hood, etc.' — it was said against it: because events are attributed to God, may He be exalted, without intermediary according to the Ash'aris, it is not valid for them to argue by substance-hood for the possibility of two effects proceeding from the truly one. So there is no way to prove the claim merely by building on obligation. His statement: 'except' by demonstrating the simplicity of the cause, which is substance-hood, even though it is not simple, for it has existence, quiddity, possibility, genus, species, and other things. If you say: It is, with all that is in it and for it, one thing to which each of the two matters is attributed, and there is no meaning to the attribution of many to the one other than this. And the conclusion: that the objection is only valid if one of the two matters is attributed to it by virtue of some of its aspects, and the other by virtue of another aspect, and here it is not so. I say: We do not grant that it is not so; for existence is nobler than possibility, and the reception of accidents because its subject is nobler than spatial occupation which implies need for space. So it is possible that the nobler is attributed to the nobler and the inferior to the inferior, as is known from their principle in explaining how contingent things proceed from the Necessary (al-Wajib).
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the issue of attributing multiple effects to a single cause. It begins with an objection: one might think that the substance's reception of accidents and its spatial occupation are two distinct effects arising from two different conditions (the state and the space). The commentator replies that the discussion is about potentiality, not actuality; the substance's potential for both is a single capacity, so only one effect is involved. Then the commentator states that the entire argument requires proving the simplicity of the cause (substance-hood), which is problematic because substance has five divisions and is not simple. He notes that this cannot be taken as an obligatory argument for the opponent. Subsequent 'قوله' sections clarify aspects: if the two potentialities do not differ in quiddity, they are one effect; the distinction between necessary and voluntary agency is reiterated; an objection that Ash'aris cannot argue from substance-hood because they attribute all events directly to God is discussed; finally, the commentator addresses the claim that substance is not simple by listing its aspects (existence, quiddity, etc.), then offers a reply: all aspects are one thing, and the hierarchy of nobility explains why noble effects are attributed to noble aspects. The argument is a dense dialectic defending the possibility of multiple effects from one simple cause.

القابلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The substance's two actual effects require two causes.
Correction: The discussion is about potentiality, not actuality. Potentiality for both is a single attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the reply to the objection about the substance's two capacities?
A) The substance actually receives accidents and is actually located in space, so two effects are needed.
B) The discussion is about potentiality, not actuality, and the potential for both is a single capacity.
C) The substance has two separate potentialities that require two causes.
D) The objection is invalid because substance-hood is simple and cannot have any attributes.

Key Takeaway
The potentiality for receiving accidents and occupying space is one capacity, not two, so it does not imply multiplicity in the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see multiple blessings in your life—health, provision, family—do you subconsciously believe they come from multiple 'causes,' or do you truly see them as flowing from the single potentiality of Allah? How does this passage's shift from actuality to potentiality urge you to unify your perception of divine action?
?
The objection assumes that distinct effects require distinct conditions. Where in your own relationship with Allah do you treat different acts of worship or different trials as if they require different 'intermediaries,' forgetting that your potential for both gratitude and patience is a single capacity from the same Lord?
?
This passage dismisses the need to prove the general possibility of multiple effects from one cause once the simplicity of the cause is established. Is your heart truly settled on the simplicity and oneness of Allah as the sole source of everything, or do you still cling to secondary causes as independent actors in your daily life?

Dialectic on the Simplicity of Substance and Multiple Effects
Further dialectic on simplicity of substance and multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further discussion on whether substance-hood is simple, and how the commentator addresses objections about Ash'ari causality and the hierarchy of nobility.

Core Idea
Even if substance were simple, multiple effects can proceed from it by virtue of different aspects of nobility.

After the initial reply, the commentator anticipates further objections: if the potentiality is one, then it is a single effect, but the objector might argue that two potentialities differ. The commentator then delves into the simplicity of the cause, the Ash'ari stance on direct divine causality, and finally a response based on the nobility of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'قوله' quotes
The commentator quotes and responds to several statements. Identify each quote and the response.
2
Analyze the objection about quiddity
The text raises the possibility that the two potentialities differ in quiddity. Notice how that would affect the argument.
3
Explore the argument about obligatory vs. voluntary causation
The commentator distinguishes between a necessitating cause (وجوب) and a voluntary chooser (اختيار). Only the former cannot produce multiple effects.
4
Consider the Ash'ari objection
Some argue that Ash'aris cannot use substance-hood as an analogy since they deny secondary causality. The commentator addresses this.
5
Reflect on the nobility hierarchy
The final point uses the principle that noble effects are attributed to noble aspects. This allows multiple effects from a single simple substance.

The Fifth Observatory - Third Goal: The attribution of multiple effects to one efficient cause. A real result. It is not said: One of them, which is the reception of accidents, is an effect of the substance (by virtue of the state) in it, and it is the accident; and the other, which is spatial occupation, is an effect of it (by virtue of the space in which it is located); so the condition has become multiple here. For we say: Our speech is not about its being a locus for the accident actually, and its being in the space actually, such that their emanation from it would be mediated by the state and the space, as you mentioned; rather, speech is about its potentiality for them, i.e., its being potential for them, which is among the accidents of its essence that are caused by it. And the truth is that this argument is not completed except by demonstrating the simplicity of the cause, which is substance-hood, and it cannot be taken as obligatory because substance according to them has five divisions, and the potential among them is primary; for after establishing this statement, we have no need to prove that possibility. So it is not objected that this argument is not obligatory due to what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned, nor is it demonstrative because they do not affirm causality except with respect to His essence, may He be exalted. His statement: 'Rather, speech is about its potentiality for them' — in it, that according to this estimation, it would be the source of a single effect, namely potentiality, unless the two potentialities are proven to differ in quiddity. His statement: 'from the accidents of its essence, etc., without mediation of the state and space even if the judgment of their existence for it is by virtue of their relation to them.' The accident between them; so the intention of that one who imagines is that if the necessitator is truly one before necessitation, it is impossible for more than one to proceed from it by necessitation. But if the chooser is truly one before choice, it is permissible for multiple effects to proceed from him by choice. This is speech with no dust on it, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'for us in proving the possibility of substance-hood, etc.' — it was said against it: because events are attributed to God, may He be exalted, without intermediary according to the Ash'aris, it is not valid for them to argue by substance-hood for the possibility of two effects proceeding from the truly one. So there is no way to prove the claim merely by building on obligation. His statement: 'except' by demonstrating the simplicity of the cause, which is substance-hood, even though it is not simple, for it has existence, quiddity, possibility, genus, species, and other things. If you say: It is, with all that is in it and for it, one thing to which each of the two matters is attributed, and there is no meaning to the attribution of many to the one other than this. And the conclusion: that the objection is only valid if one of the two matters is attributed to it by virtue of some of its aspects, and the other by virtue of another aspect, and here it is not so. I say: We do not grant that it is not so; for existence is nobler than possibility, and the reception of accidents because its subject is nobler than spatial occupation which implies need for space. So it is possible that the nobler is attributed to the nobler and the inferior to the inferior, as is known from their principle in explaining how contingent things proceed from the Necessary (al-Wajib).

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the issue of attributing multiple effects to a single cause. It begins with an objection: one might think that the substance's reception of accidents and its spatial occupation are two distinct effects arising from two different conditions (the state and the space). The commentator replies that the discussion is about potentiality, not actuality; the substance's potential for both is a single capacity, so only one effect is involved. Then the commentator states that the entire argument requires proving the simplicity of the cause (substance-hood), which is problematic because substance has five divisions and is not simple. He notes that this cannot be taken as an obligatory argument for the opponent. Subsequent 'قوله' sections clarify aspects: if the two potentialities do not differ in quiddity, they are one effect; the distinction between necessary and voluntary agency is reiterated; an objection that Ash'aris cannot argue from substance-hood because they attribute all events directly to God is discussed; finally, the commentator addresses the claim that substance is not simple by listing its aspects (existence, quiddity, etc.), then offers a reply: all aspects are one thing, and the hierarchy of nobility explains why noble effects are attributed to noble aspects. The argument is a dense dialectic defending the possibility of multiple effects from one simple cause.

The First Philosophers' Proof: Distinct Sourcehoods
Philosophers present first proof against multiple effects from one cause, with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first of the philosophers' three proofs against multiple effects from a single true cause, including the role of distinct sourcehoods, the obligatory interpretation, and the discussion on atomism.

Core Idea
Distinct concepts of sourcehood force multiplicity in the truly one cause.

The philosophers argue that if a single cause produces two effects, the sourcehood for each effect must be conceptually distinct, leading to multiplicity in the cause. This passage presents the first of three such proofs, along with important clarifications about atomism and the nature of relations. As you read, pay attention to how the author sets up the problem and then develops the core logical dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the context setting
Read the opening lines describing the body, form, matter, and the denial of the individual atom. This sets the stage for the proof.
The context is important for understanding the philosophers' metaphysical framework.
2
Identify the core argument
Find the first proof: if a truly one cause is a source for A and B, then the sourcehoods must be distinct, leading to a dilemma about entering the one.
This is the heart of the proof.
3
Examine the obligatory interpretation
Note the discussion of taking the argument as obligatory based on substance as a highest genus. Why might this be problematic?
The commentary indicates weakness in this approach.
4
Consider the atom and intellectual parts
Read the section on the individual atom and intellectual parts. How does this relate to the possibility of multiple effects from a simple cause?
This shows a possible counterargument.
5
Review the philological note
Notice the comment about the phrasing of 'enter into it' and the preference for 'for if they enter'.
This is a minor but illustrative point about textual precision.

(و) بيان كون الأمرين (أي القابليتين اللتين هما الأثران) وجوديين. قيل : ويمكن أخذه إلزامياً لأنهما من النسب والإضافات التي لا وجود لها عند المتكلمين بخلاف الحكماء. The Fifth Observatory - Third Goal: The attribution of multiple effects to one efficient cause. For spatial occupation and the inherence of these accidents is the body by virtue of its form and matter, and there is no existence for the individual atom according to them. And the demonstration that the two matters (i.e., the two potentialities which are the two effects) are existent. It was said: And it can be taken as obligatory because they are among relations and additions that have no existence according to the theologians, unlike the philosophers. And the demonstration of the absence of multiplicity of instrument and condition in the emanation of the two potentialities from substance-hood, and it is problematic. The philosophers argued against the possibility by three aspects: The first: if the truly one were a source for (A) and for (B) for example, then sourcehood-for-(A) would be other than sourcehood-for-(B); because each can be conceived without the other. For if they — i.e., these two concepts — enter into the truly one, or one of them enters into it, it would necessitate... His statement: 'taking it as obligatory' based on their statement that substance is a highest genus, so it is simple. His statement: 'for the individual atom' so that it is said that it is simple and two effects proceeded from it. His statement: 'it was said it can be taken as obligatory, etc.' — in it there is an indication of its weakness; because they did not affirm the existence of all relations and additions. His statement: 'and it is problematic' i.e., the demonstration of the three matters. His statement: 'then sourcehood would be...' i.e., in the relative sense as is immediately understood, or the necessary consequence of being a source, so that the answer mentioned in the text is directed, and the mentioned answer is refuted by what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) stated in his saying: 'for if it is said, etc.' So the hesitation in their entering and exiting is only for the sake of apparent meaning; otherwise, exiting is determined according to this meaning. So what is said that on the estimation of the difference of the two sourcehoods, multiplicity would follow in the truly one, and this is contrary to the assumption, so the mentioned argument is based on concession and is not valid. His statement: 'i.e., these two concepts' indicates that the author took liberty by applying the rule of reference to the pronoun where he made it manifest; otherwise, the correct phrasing would be 'for if they enter' and that the masculine form is by interpreting sourcehood as a concept. His statement: 'and there is no existence for the individual atom according to them. It was said: Even if its existence is assumed, it is possible for it to have intellectual parts, and the intellectual parts, even if their existence is the same as the existence of the person, so the source would be simple in the external, except that it is possible for them to be principles of external effects; for example, it is possible for Zayd, by virtue of being an animal, to be a principle of walking, and by virtue of being human, to be a principle of wonder, even if his simplicity in the external is assumed. And how not? For multiplicity by virtue of intellectual parts is not inferior to multiplicity by virtue of external intellectual aspects.' His statement: 'it was said and it can be taken as obligatory' — it is heard from him (may God have mercy on him) that it is an indication of weakness; because they do not affirm the existence of all relations and additions such that they encompass potentialities. His statement: 'then sourcehood-for-(1) would be other than sourcehood-for-(B)', so multiplicity would follow in the truly one, and this is contrary to the assumption, along with the fact that if the two sourcehoods enter into it... etc. His statement: 'for if they enter into it (them)' in the text's phrasing is weak; because the position is not the position of a separate pronoun. The preferable phrasing is 'for if they enter'.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof against the possibility of multiple effects from a single true cause, along with accompanying commentary. The passage begins by noting that the body is by form and matter, and there is no individual atom according to the theologians. Then it states that the two potentialities (effects) are existent, and an objection is raised: the argument can be taken as obligatory because these are relations that theologians deny existence to, unlike philosophers. The core proof is the first of three aspects: if a truly one cause were a source for A and B, then sourcehood-for-A would be different from sourcehood-for-B, since each can be conceived without the other. If both concepts enter the one, it would be multiple; if one enters, the other is external. The commentary then discusses various points: the argument can be taken as obligatory based on substance being a highest genus (thus simple); the individual atom could have intellectual parts allowing multiple effects; the weakness of taking the argument as obligatory because theologians don't affirm all relations; the need to show the absence of instrument and conditions; and a philological note about the phrasing of 'enter into it'. The overall argument is that the philosophers' proof forces the opponent to concede multiplicity in the cause, or else the argument is based on a false assumption.

Key Terms
الواحد الحقيقي
مصدرية
القابليتين
الجوهر الفرد
النسب والإضافات
الآلة والشرط
الأجزاء العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof concerns physical instruments or parts in the cause.
Correction: The proof is about conceptual sourcehoods (concepts of being a source for each effect), not physical instruments. The argument is logical, not physical.

Checkpoint
What is the first of the three aspects by which the philosophers argue against multiple effects from one true cause?
A) The cause must have multiple instruments, which is impossible.
B) The two effects would require two different conditions, leading to multiplicity in the cause.
C) The sourcehood for A and sourcehood for B are distinct concepts, so if the cause is truly one, both concepts cannot enter it without multiplicity.
D) The cause is simple in the external, but intellectual parts allow multiple effects.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that distinct sourcehoods necessarily imply multiplicity in the cause, which is the crux of the philosophers' position.

The Second Proof: Empirical Evidence for Unity of Cause and Effect
Al-Iji uses empirical observation to prove that diverse effects require diverse causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji uses empirical observation and a rational principle to prove that distinct effects require distinct causes, establishing the unity of cause.

Core Idea
Different effects demand different causes; this is ingrained in the rational mind.

Al-Iji now presents a second, empirical proof. He begins with a simple observation: water cools and fire heats. From this difference in effects, he infers a difference in the causes. This inference is not merely inductive; it is grounded in a rational principle that is deeply ingrained in the intellect. Let us examine how this principle leads to the desired conclusion about the unity of the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its argument. Notice the structure: observation, inference, principle, conclusion.
The passage is concise but contains a complete logical argument.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Find the observation about water and fire. What effects do they produce?
The observation is the starting point of the proof.
3
Understand the rational principle
The passage states that it is ingrained in the intellects that different effects must come from different causes. This is the key principle.
This principle is not derived from observation but is a priori.
4
Follow the contrapositive
The passage concludes that if the effect is one, the cause is one. This is the contrapositive of the earlier statement.
This establishes the unity of the cause.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this proof is important for tawhid. It shows that the universe's diverse effects point to a single cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أنا لما رأينا الماء يوجب البرودة، والنار توجب السخونة، قطعنا بأن طبيعة النار غير طبيعة الماء ضرورة (أي قطعاً يقينياً لا شبهة فيه، فقد استدللنا باختلاف الأثر وتعدده على اختلاف المؤثر وتعدده . فلولا أنه مركوز في العقول أن اختلاف الأثر وتعدده لا يكون إلا باختلاف المؤثر) وتعدده ، ( لما كان ( الأمر ( كذلك ) ، فظهر أنه كلما تعدد المعلول تعدد العلة وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كلما اتحدت العلة اتحد المعلول، وهو المطلوب
The second proof: Because we have seen that water causes coldness and fire causes heat, we are certain that the nature of fire is different from the nature of water by necessity (i.e., with certainty and without doubt). Thus we have inferred from the difference and multiplicity of the effect to the difference and multiplicity of the cause. If it were not ingrained in the intellects that difference and multiplicity of effect occur only with difference and multiplicity of cause, then it would not be so. So it becomes clear that whenever the effect is multiple, the cause is multiple, and the contrapositive is also true: whenever the cause is one, the effect is one. And that is what was desired.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is empirical. It begins with an observation: we see that water causes coldness, fire causes heat. From this difference in effects, we infer a difference in the natures of the causes. This inference is based on a rational intuition that is ingrained in the intellects: that the difference and multiplicity of effects can only come from a difference and multiplicity of causes. If this intuition were not present, we would not make such a judgment. Therefore, the principle is established: whenever the effect is multiple, the cause is multiple. By contrapositive, whenever the cause is one, the effect is one. This is the desired conclusion. The proof relies on a universal rational principle, not merely on empirical generalization.

Correction: The text explicitly states that the inference is based on a rational principle ingrained in the intellect, not just on repeated observation.

The Third Proof and the Relational Nature of Sourcehood
Al-Iji presents a reductio proof and a relational answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction that arises if a single real being produces two distinct effects, and how the relational interpretation of sourcehood resolves it.

Core Idea
A single source producing two effects leads to self-contradiction unless sourcehood is merely relational.

In this passage, al-Iji presents a crisp reductio argument: if a single entity produced two different effects, it would simultaneously be the source of each and of what is not each—a direct contradiction. He then offers a crucial answer to the first proof: sourcehood is not a real attribute but a relational matter. This distinction preserves divine simplicity while allowing causal multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the reductio structure. Notice how it assumes a single source for two effects and derives a contradiction.
The proof uses letters (a) and (b) to represent two distinct effects.
2
Analyze the contradiction
Identify why the scenario is impossible: if the source produces (a), it produces what is not (a) because (b) is not (a). Similarly for (b). This yields a logical contradiction.
The contradiction is that the same thing is both itself and not itself.
3
Examine the answer to the first proof
Study the brief answer: 'Sourcehood is a relational matter.' Reflect on how this avoids composition in the essence.
The answer is short but profound; it shifts the discussion from real attributes to relations.
4
Connect to divine simplicity
Consider why this response is important for the doctrine of divine simplicity. Relations do not add real plurality to God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث، أنه لو كان ) الواحد الحقيقي ( مصدراً لأثرين) كـ (ا) و (ب) مثلاً، لكان مصدراً لـ (ا) ، ولما ليس (۱)؛ لأن (ب) ليس (ا) ، ولكان أيضاً مصدراً لـ (ب)، ولما ليس لـ (ب) (وأنه تناقض . والجواب عن الأول : المصدرية أمر اعتباري ) أي
The third proof: If the single real being were a source for two effects, such as (a) and (b), then it would be a source for (a) and for what is not (a), because (b) is not (a); and it would also be a source for (b) and for what is not (b). And that is a contradiction. The answer to the first proof: Sourcehood is a relational matter (i.e., it is something relational).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which is a direct reductio ad absurdum. If a single real being produces two distinct effects a and b, then it is the source of a and also the source of what is not a (since b is not a). Similarly, it would be the source of b and what is not b. This yields a contradiction: the same thing is both itself and not itself. Therefore, the assumption is impossible. After presenting the three proofs, the author gives the answer to the first proof: sourcehood is a relational concept (أمر اعتباري). This is a key response: the sourcehood of a and b are not real attributes added to the essence but are relations. This avoids composition because relations do not entail real multiplicity in the essence. The answer is brief and likely continues in the following text.

Key Terms
مصدرية
أمر اعتباري
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof alone suffices to prove that a single being cannot produce multiple effects; therefore divine simplicity is untenable.
Correction: The answer shows that sourcehood is relational, so the apparent multiplicity of effects does not require real multiplicity in the essence. The contradiction is avoided by recognizing that sourcehood is not a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first proof, what is the nature of sourcehood (المصدرية)?
A) A real attribute added to the essence
B) A relational matter (أمر اعتباري)
C) An accident inherent in the agent
D) A mental construct with no basis

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that a single real being cannot produce two distinct effects without contradiction unless sourcehood is understood as a relational concept, preserving divine simplicity.

Empirical Evidence for Distinct Causes
Al-Jurjani argues from observed differences between water and fire to the rational principle that distinct effects require distinct causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical observation of differing effects (like water and fire) leads to the rational principle that distinct effects require distinct causes, and its contrapositive.

Core Idea
Different effects demand different causes.

Al-Jurjani presents the second argument based on everyday experience. We observe that water cools and fire heats, and our minds immediately judge that their natures must be different. This illustrates a foundational rational principle: multiplicity in effects indicates multiplicity in causes. The argument then draws the contrapositive, confirming that a single cause produces only a single effect. This passage demonstrates how kalam uses empirical evidence to support rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses in the Arabic text.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Locate the water and fire example. Understand what is being observed and the immediate judgment made.
This is the basis of the argument.
3
Extract the rational principle
The passage states that it is ingrained in intellects that difference and multiplicity of effect cannot occur without difference and multiplicity of cause. Identify this principle and its formulation.
4
Understand the contrapositive
The principle is reversed by contrapositive to state: whenever the cause is one, the effect is one. Follow the logical reversal.
This is the desired conclusion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters for the broader discussion of causality in kalam. How does it support the position that a single simple cause cannot produce multiple effects?
Connect to the overall debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه ( الثاني: أنا لما رأينا الماء يوجب البرودة، والنار توجب السخونة، قطعنا بأن طبيعة النار غير طبيعة الماء ضرورة (أي قطعاً يقينياً لا شبهة فيه، فقد استدللنا باختلاف الأثر وتعدده على اختلاف المؤثر وتعدده . فلولا أنه مركوز في العقول أن اختلاف الأثر وتعدده لا يكون إلا باختلاف المؤثر) وتعدده ، ( لما كان ( الأمر ( كذلك ) ، فظهر أنه كلما تعدد المعلول تعدد العلة وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كلما اتحدت العلة اتحد المعلول، وهو المطلوب
The second argument: When we see that water causes cold and fire causes heat, we judge necessarily that the nature of fire is different from the nature of water, i.e., with certain and unquestionable certainty. For we have inferred from the difference and multiplicity of the effect to the difference and multiplicity of the cause. Were it not ingrained in intellects that difference and multiplicity of effect only occur due to difference and multiplicity of the cause, the matter would not be so. Thus it becomes clear that whenever the effect is multiple, the cause is multiple, and it is reflected by contrapositive to our saying: whenever the cause is one, the effect is one, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) based on empirical observation. The author points to common experience: water causes cold, fire causes heat. We know with certainty that their natures are different because their effects differ. This demonstrates a rational principle ingrained in minds: difference and multiplicity in effects indicates difference and multiplicity in causes. The contrapositive of this principle is then drawn: if the cause is one, the effect must be one. This is the desired conclusion (المطلوب) for the overall discussion, which aims to prove that a single simple cause cannot produce multiple effects.

Key Terms

المؤثر
العلة
المعلول

Misconception: A single cause can produce multiple distinct effects through different aspects.
Correction: The argument from common experience demonstrates that the rational principle ingrained in minds is that distinct effects necessarily imply distinct causes. Therefore, a single cause cannot produce genuinely distinct effects.

Key Takeaway

Source-Hood as a Relational Concept
Al-Jurjani argues that source-hood is a relational concept and regress in such matters is not impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why source-hood (مصدرية) is considered a relational concept (اعتباري) that does not entail real causal regress.

Core Idea
Source-hood is mental, not real—no regress of real causes follows.

In responding to the first argument, Al-Jurjani argues that source-hood is not a real entity but a relational concept. This distinction blocks the claim of infinite regress. The following passage lays out this key point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the claim that source-hood is i'tibari.
The text uses parentheses for inserted comments.
2
Identify the claim about source-hood
The passage asserts that source-hood is a relational matter (أمر اعتباري) with no external existence. Find this statement and understand its implication.
3
Understand why no real regress follows
Since source-hood does not exist externally, it does not need a cause. Therefore, there is no chain of real causes. Even if a regress of relational concepts is granted, it is not impossible because it is not a regress of real existents.
Contrast this with regress in real entities, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول : المصدرية أمر اعتباري ) أي نختار أن المصدريتين خارجتان عن الواحد الحقيقي، إلا أن المصدرية لكونها من الأمور الإضافية التي لا وجود لها في الخارج غير محتاجة إلى علة توجدها ( فلا تكون الذات مصدراً لها؛ لأن المحتاج إلى الموجد ما له وجود، وحينئذ فلا يكون هناك مصدرية أخرى حتى تتسلسل المصدريات . وإن سلمنا ) تسلسها، فالتسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية غير ممتنع فإن قيل : لا شك أن العلة الموجودة يجب أن تكون موجودة قبل المعلول قبلية بالذات وأنه يجب أن يكون لها خصوصية ذلك المعلول ليست لأن مصدريته ليس نفسه ولا جزءه ؛ لكونه نسبة خارجة عن الطرفين فيكون له مصدرية أخرى ويتسلسل . قوله: (غير محتاجة إلى علة توجدها وإن كانت محتاجة إلى علة للاتصاف بها، وهو البسيط الحقيقي، فكونها منتزعة من نفسه باعتبار استتباعها للأثر. قوله : ( حتى تتسلسل المصدريات أي يحصل سلسلتها . قوله : ( وإن سلمنا تسلسلها) يعني أن التسليم ليس راجعاً إلى كون الذات مصدراً لها، كما هو السابق إلى الفهم؛ لأنه لا يمكن حينئذ القول بأنه تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية، بل إلى ما يترتب عليه أعني التسلسل المشار إليه بقوله : حتى تتسلسل المصدريات أي إن سلمنا حصول سلسلة المصدريات بأن ينتزع العقل من كل مصدرية مصدرية أخرى نسبة بينها وبين البسيط الحقيقي، فهذا التسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية وهو غير ممتنع لأنه ينقطع بحسب انقطاع اعتبار العقل . قوله : ( فإن قيل تحرير الدليل المذكور بحيث يندفع عنه الجواب المذكور. قوله : (خصوصية) ليس المراد الأمر الإضافي، فيرد عليه ما يرد على المصدرية، بل ما العلة يقتضي وجود المعلول على نحو خاص، لم يقل : ولا شك أنه موجود؛ لأنه العلة في الحقيقة، كما في تقرير شارح التجريد لأنه لا حاجة إليه ؛ إذ لزم أن لا يكون الفاعل واحداً من لأجله اعتبارية حقيقية لا فرضية محضة والتسلسل فيها محال قطعاً . وأجيب : بأنه لا تسلسل إذ ليس لها وجود حتى يطلب العلة لوجودها ولا يلزم أن يكون اتصاف العلة الموجبة لها ممكناً خاصاً حتى يطلب علة الاتصاف فعلى كلا التقديرين لا يحتاج إلى مصدرية أخرى وفيه ما أشرنا إليه في بحث زيادة وجود الواجب . قوله : ( فالتسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية غير ممتنع فيه بحث؛ لأن المصدرية على تقدير أن يحتاج إلى مصدرية أخرى ويتسلسل يرد أن يقال : مجموع المصدريات الغير المتناهية بحيث لا يشذ عنها شيء يحتاج إلى مصدرية أخرى خارجة عن المجموع، فلا يكون الجميع جميعاً. والحاصل أنه لو سلم عدم جريان برهان التطبيق هاهنا امتنع بوجه آخر . قوله : ( وأنه يجب أن يكون لها خصوصية فإن قلت: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الخصوصية راجعة إلى المعلول بأن يكون لماهية المعلول خصوصية مع علة معينة ليست لها مع غيرها،
And the answer to the first [argument]: Source-hood (مصدرية) is a relational matter (أمر اعتباري). That is, we choose that the two source-hoods are external to the truly simple [entity], but source-hood, because it belongs to relational things that have no existence in the external, does not need a cause to bring it into existence. So the essence is not a source for it, because what needs a bringer into existence is that which has existence. Thus there is no other source-hood such that source-hoods would regress. And if we grant its regress, then regress in relational matters is not impossible. For it is said: There is no doubt that the existing cause must exist prior to the effect with essential priority, and that it must have a particularity (خصوصية) of that effect that is not... because its being a source is not itself nor a part of it, being a relation external to both parties, so it would have another source-hood and regress. His saying: 'not needing a cause to bring it into existence, although it needs a cause for being described by it, and that is the truly simple; so it is derived from itself by virtue of its entailment of the effect.' His saying: 'so that source-hoods regress' i.e., its chain occurs. His saying: 'And if we grant its regress' means that the granting does not refer to the essence being a source for them, as might be understood, because then one could not say it is a regress in relational matters, but rather to what follows from it, namely the regress indicated by his saying 'so that source-hoods regress'; i.e., if we grant the occurrence of a chain of source-hoods by the intellect extracting from each source-hood another source-hood, a relation between it and the truly simple, then this regress is in relational matters, and it is not impossible because it terminates according to the termination of the intellect's consideration. His saying: 'for it is said' is a refinement of the mentioned argument such that the mentioned answer is repelled. His saying: 'particularity' does not mean the relational matter, lest what applies to source-hood applies to it, but rather that the cause necessitates the existence of the effect in a specific manner. He did not say: and there is no doubt that it exists, because it is the cause in reality, as in the explanation of Sharh al-Tajrid, for there is no need for it; otherwise it would follow that the agent is not one... for the sake of it being a real relational matter, not a mere supposition, and regress in it is absolutely impossible. And it was answered: There is no regress because it has no existence such that a cause for its existence is sought, nor does it require that the attribute of the necessitating cause be a special possibility such that a cause for the attribute is sought. So in both estimations, it does not need another source-hood, and in it is what we indicated in the discussion on the increase of the existence of the Necessary. His saying: 'so regress in relational matters is not impossible' is debatable; because source-hood, on the supposition that it needs another source-hood and regresses, it could be said: the infinite totality of source-hoods, such that nothing is excluded from it, would need another source-hood outside the totality, so the totality would not be all. The conclusion is that even if the argument of تطبيق (correspondence) is not applicable here, it is impossible by another way. His saying: 'and that it must have a particularity' – if you ask: why is it not permissible that the particularity refer to the effect, such that the essence of the effect has a particularity with a certain cause that it does not have with another?
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage provides the response (الجواب) to the first argument (الوجه الأول). The responder argues that 'source-hood' (مصدرية) is a relational (اعتباري) matter, not a real entity. Therefore, it does not require a cause to bring it into existence. The two source-hoods for effects (a) and (b) are said to be external to the simple essence, but since they are mere relations, no regress of real causes occurs. Even if a regress of relational concepts is granted, it is not impossible because such regress is only in the intellect and terminates when the intellect stops considering it. The passage then includes an objection (فإن قيل) that attempts to refine the argument to avoid this answer, claiming that the particularity (خصوصية) of the effect requires a real cause. The response counters that particularity is not relational in the same way, but rather pertains to the causal necessity. Further, it argues that there is no infinite regress because these relational matters have no real existence that would require a cause. A debate follows about whether regress in اعتباري matters is truly impossible, with some pointing out that an infinite set of source-hoods would still require an external source, but the answer is that such regress is not actual. The passage concludes with a discussion of particularity and whether it could belong to the effect rather than the cause.

Key Terms
المصدرية
الاعتباري
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any infinite regress is impossible in all cases.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between regress in real existents (impossible) and regress in relational/mental matters (not impossible).

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first argument, why does source-hood (مصدرية) not entail a real causal regress?
A) Because source-hood is a real entity that does not need a cause.
B) Because source-hood is a relational matter with no external existence.
C) Because the simple entity cannot be a source for anything.
D) Because the regress terminates after two steps.

Key Takeaway
Source-hood is a relational concept that does not require a cause, so no real regress follows.

Objection and Further Discussion on Regress in I'tibari Matters
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and answers, with further discussion on regress in relational matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that particularity (خصوصية) seems to require a real cause and the response that no regress occurs because these are not real existents.

Core Idea
Particularity is not a relational concept; still, no regress occurs because it has no existence.

After the initial response, an objector refines the argument, claiming that the particularity of the effect demands a real cause. Al-Jurjani and his commentators respond by clarifying that particularity is not a mere relation and that even then, no regress follows. This passage explores that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector argues that the cause must have a particularity for the effect, which is not relational, thus needing another source-hood. Find this objection.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قيل'.
2
Understand the concept of particularity
Particularity (خصوصية) is explained as not being a relational matter but a real necessity of the cause. The passage clarifies this distinction.
3
See the response that there is no regress
The response argues that these matters have no existence, so no cause is sought. There is no regress because there is no real chain.
The response also notes that even if regress in i'tibari matters is granted, it is not impossible.
4
Debate on regress in i'tibari matters
A further discussion points out that an infinite set of source-hoods would need an external source, but the answer is that such regress is only mental.
This shows the depth of the dialectic.

والجواب عن الأول : المصدرية أمر اعتباري ) أي نختار أن المصدريتين خارجتان عن الواحد الحقيقي، إلا أن المصدرية لكونها من الأمور الإضافية التي لا وجود لها في الخارج غير محتاجة إلى علة توجدها ( فلا تكون الخصوصية راجعة إلى المعلول بأن يكون لماهية المعلول خصوصية مع علة معينة ليست لها مع غيرها،
And the answer to the first [argument]: Source-hood (مصدرية) is a relational matter (أمر اعتباري). That is, we choose that the two source-hoods are external to the truly simple [entity], but source-hood, because it belongs to relational things that have no existence in the external, does not need a cause to bring it into existence. So the essence is not a source for it, because what needs a bringer into existence is that which has existence. Thus there is no other source-hood such that source-hoods would regress. And if we grant its regress, then regress in relational matters is not impossible. For it is said: There is no doubt that the existing cause must exist prior to the effect with essential priority, and that it must have a particularity (خصوصية) of that effect that is not... because its being a source is not itself nor a part of it, being a relation external to both parties, so it would have another source-hood and regress. His saying: 'not needing a cause to bring it into existence, although it needs a cause for being described by it, and that is the truly simple; so it is derived from itself by virtue of its entailment of the effect.' His saying: 'so that source-hoods regress' i.e., its chain occurs. His saying: 'And if we grant its regress' means that the granting does not refer to the essence being a source for them, as might be understood, because then one could not say it is a regress in relational matters, but rather to what follows from it, namely the regress indicated by his saying 'so that source-hoods regress'; i.e., if we grant the occurrence of a chain of source-hoods by the intellect extracting from each source-hood another source-hood, a relation between it and the truly simple, then this regress is in relational matters, and it is not impossible because it terminates according to the termination of the intellect's consideration. His saying: 'for it is said' is a refinement of the mentioned argument such that the mentioned answer is repelled. His saying: 'particularity' does not mean the relational matter, lest what applies to source-hood applies to it, but rather that the cause necessitates the existence of the effect in a specific manner. He did not say: and there is no doubt that it exists, because it is the cause in reality, as in the explanation of Sharh al-Tajrid, for there is no need for it; otherwise it would follow that the agent is not one... for the sake of it being a real relational matter, not a mere supposition, and regress in it is absolutely impossible. And it was answered: There is no regress because it has no existence such that a cause for its existence is sought, nor does it require that the attribute of the necessitating cause be a special possibility such that a cause for the attribute is sought. So in both estimations, it does not need another source-hood, and in it is what we indicated in the discussion on the increase of the existence of the Necessary. His saying: 'so regress in relational matters is not impossible' is debatable; because source-hood, on the supposition that it needs another source-hood and regresses, it could be said: the infinite totality of source-hoods, such that nothing is excluded from it, would need another source-hood outside the totality, so the totality would not be all. The conclusion is that even if the argument of تطبيق (correspondence) is not applicable here, it is impossible by another way. His saying: 'and that it must have a particularity' – if you ask: why is it not permissible that the particularity refer to the effect, such that the essence of the effect has a particularity with a certain cause that it does not have with another?
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage provides the response (الجواب) to the first argument (الوجه الأول). The responder argues that 'source-hood' (مصدرية) is a relational (اعتباري) matter, not a real entity. Therefore, it does not require a cause to bring it into existence. The two source-hoods for effects (a) and (b) are said to be external to the simple essence, but since they are mere relations, no regress of real causes occurs. Even if a regress of relational concepts is granted, it is not impossible because such regress is only in the intellect and terminates when the intellect stops considering it. The passage then includes an objection (فإن قيل) that attempts to refine the argument to avoid this answer, claiming that the particularity (خصوصية) of the effect requires a real cause. The response counters that particularity is not relational in the same way, but rather pertains to the causal necessity. Further, it argues that there is no infinite regress because these relational matters have no real existence that would require a cause. A debate follows about whether regress in اعتباري matters is truly impossible, with some pointing out that an infinite set of source-hoods would still require an external source, but the answer is that such regress is not actual. The passage concludes with a discussion of particularity and whether it could belong to the effect rather than the cause.

الخصوصية
الاعتباري
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Particularity is just another relational concept like source-hood.
Correction: The passage clarifies that particularity is not a relational matter but a real necessity of the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the objection, why does particularity (خصوصية) not lead to an infinite regress of source-hoods?
A) Because particularity is a relational concept that does not need a cause.
B) Because the cause does not actually have a particularity.
C) Because the regress would be in matters that have no existence, so no cause is needed.
D) Because the regress terminates after two steps.

Key Takeaway
The objection based on particularity does not force a real infinite regress because the entities involved have no external existence; regress in mental matters is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How do you react when you realize that your intellectual attempts to fully understand God's actions are ultimately mental constructs that terminate? Does that humility unsettle you or free you?
?
In what areas of your life do you demand a complete logical chain from cause to effect, refusing to accept that some things are beyond your conceptual grasp? How might that be a form of hidden pride?
?
If sourcehood and particularity are mental concepts that have no real existence apart from God's simple essence, what does that imply about your need to 'figure out' how God's attributes relate? Can you surrender your need for a perfect mental model?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
The Necessity of Particularity for Each Effect
Author explains the necessity and nature of particularity for each effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every effect requires a particularity from its cause and why this particularity does not lead to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
A cause must have a special relation to each effect, but this relation is not a separate existent.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection in causal reasoning: how can a cause be the source of a specific effect without having a special relation to it? He explains that this 'particularity' is not a separate entity added to the cause, but a real conceptual matter that does not require an infinite chain of such relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The initial objection states that a cause must have a particularity for its effect, otherwise the cause cannot be the source of that specific effect. Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the problem.
Notice the term 'خصوصية' (particularity) is introduced.
2
Understand the definition of particularity
The author clarifies that particularity is not the relational 'source-ness' but the cause's necessitating the effect in a specific manner. This avoids the problem of infinite regress.
The distinction between 'source-ness' and 'particularity' is crucial.
3
Address the infinite regress concern
The answer shows that since particularity is a conceptual matter derived by the intellect from the cause's relation to its effect, it does not need a separate cause, thus no regress.
Conceptual matters (الأمور الاعتبارية) do not require real existence.

أحدهما بحسب
الخصوصية الموجودة على الفرض فيحتاج إلى خصوصية أخرى، ويتسلسل فليتأمل. قوله: (ولهذا قيل: إن هذا الحكم كأنه قريب من (الوضوح هذا الكلام ذكره شارح
For it is said: There is no doubt that the existing cause must be prior to the effect by essential priority, and that it must have a particularity for that effect, not because its source-ness is itself or part of it, because it is a relation external to both, so it would have another source-ness and chain.
His saying: (Not in need of a cause to bring it into existence) although it is in need of a cause for the attribution to it, which is the true simple, so its being derived from itself in consideration of its being subsequent to the effect.
His saying: (So that the source-nesses would form a chain) i.e., its chain comes to be.
His saying: (And if we grant its chaining) means that the granting does not refer to the Essence being a source for them, as is the initial understanding; because then it cannot be said that it is a chain in conceptual matters, but rather to what follows from it, namely the chain indicated by his saying: 'so that the source-nesses would form a chain', i.e., if we grant the occurrence of the chain of source-nesses by the intellect deriving from each source-ness another source-ness as a relation between it and the true simple, then this chain is in conceptual matters and it is not impossible because it is cut off according to the cessation of the intellect's consideration.
His saying: (For it is said, refining the mentioned proof so that the mentioned answer is repelled. His saying: (Particularity) does not mean the relational matter, otherwise what applies to source-ness would apply to it, but rather [it means] that the cause necessitates the existence of the effect in a particular manner. He did not say: There is no doubt that it exists; because it is the cause in reality, as in the exposition of Sharh al-Tajrid because there is no need for it; for then it would necessitate that the agent is not one... for the sake of [it is] real conceptual [matters] not merely hypothetical, and chaining in them is absolutely impossible. And it was answered: There is no chain, because they have no existence such that a cause for their existence is sought, nor does it follow that the attribution of the necessitating cause to them is a particular possible [matter] such that a cause for the attribution is sought, so on both estimates there is no need for another source-ness, and in it is what we indicated in the discussion of the increase of the existence of the Necessary.
His saying: (So chaining in conceptual matters is not impossible) contains a discussion; because source-ness, on the supposition that it needs another source-ness and chains, it could be said: The set of infinite source-nesses such that nothing escapes it needs another source-ness outside the set, so the totality is not all. The conclusion: even if the application of the proof of correspondence (برهان التطبيق) is not admitted here, it is impossible by another aspect.
His saying: (And that it must have a particularity) for you say: Why is it not permitted that the particularity belongs to the effect, such that the quiddity of the effect has a particularity with a specific cause that it does not have with another? ... (continues with the argument that without a special relation the effect would not be determined, leading to the conclusion that a single agent cannot have multiple effects without multiple aspects, and thus cannot be one from all aspects. The passage then discusses further objections and responses, including reference to al-Dawani's argument, and concludes that this judgment is nearly obvious but people overlook the meaning of unity.)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a sustained objection-and-answer argument. It begins with an objection: the cause must have a particularity (خصوصية) for each effect, otherwise it could not be the cause. The author then refines the definition of 'particularity' to mean the cause's necessitating the effect in a specific manner, not a relational matter. The objection of infinite regress is rejected because the particularity is a real conceptual matter, not a separate existing thing. The core argument then shows: if the agent is truly one (simple), it can have only one particularity. If multiple effects occur, the agent would need multiple particularities, which requires multiple aspects in its essence. Since a truly one agent has no aspects, it cannot have multiple particularities, so it cannot be the cause of multiple distinct effects. Therefore, the agent cannot be one from all aspects. The author notes that this is nearly obvious, but people overlook the meaning of unity. The passage continues to address further objections: that the particularity might belong to the effect, not the cause, and that the agent's simplicity might still be sufficient. The response reiterates that without multiple aspects, there cannot be multiple distinct particularities. The last part discusses what would follow if there were multiple aspects, leading to another infinite regress, and comments on al-Dawani's critique.

Key Terms
الخصوصية
المصدرية
الأمور الاعتبارية
العلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The particularity (خصوصية) is a separate entity that would require another cause, leading to an infinite regress.
Correction: The particularity is not a separate entity but a conceptual matter derived from the cause's necessitation of the effect. It does not exist independently, so no infinite regress occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the particularity of a cause for its effect not lead to an infinite regress?
A) Because the particularity is the cause itself
B) Because the particularity is a conceptual matter with no real existence, so it does not need a cause
C) Because the regress stops at the first source-ness
D) Because the cause is simple and cannot have multiple particularities

Key Takeaway
Every effect requires a particularity from its cause, but this particularity is a conceptual matter that does not cause an infinite regress.

When you observe causes and effects in your daily life, do you instinctively attribute the 'source-ness' as a real quality in the cause? How does realizing it is merely conceptual change the way you see Allah as the sole true Cause?
?
If the particularity between a cause and its effect is only a conceptual matter derived by your intellect, then what does that say about your certainty in secondary causes? Will you now rely more on the Creator, the only real Cause, and less on created means?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
12 min
The Impossibility of Multiple Effects from a Single Simple Agent
Author proves that a single simple agent cannot have multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that a truly simple agent cannot produce more than one effect, because it would require multiple particularities, which necessitate multiple aspects in the agent.

Core Idea
True oneness limits a cause to a single effect.

This passage contains the central argument: if an agent is truly one in every respect, it can have only one particularity, and hence only one effect. To produce multiple effects, it would need multiple particularities, which require multiple aspects or parts, contradicting its oneness. The author also responds to the objection that particularity might belong to the effect rather than the cause.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the main argument
The author argues that a single simple agent has only one essence, so it can have only one particularity. Without multiple particularities, it cannot be the cause of multiple distinct effects.
The key premise: a simple agent has only one essence, thus only one particularity.
2
Consider the counterargument
The objection is that particularity might be in the effect, not the cause. The author responds that even if so, the effect's quiddity would have a specific relation to the cause, but the cause itself would still need to have a corresponding particularity.
This objection is refuted by showing it shifts the problem without solving it.
3
Understand the conclusion
The agent cannot be one from all aspects if it produces multiple effects; this shows the necessity of multiple aspects in the cause for multiple effects.
The author emphasizes that this truth is nearly obvious, but people overlook the meaning of true unity.

For it is said: There is no doubt that the existing cause must be prior to the effect by essential priority, and that it must have a particularity for that effect, not because its source-ness is itself or part of it, because it is a relation external to both, so it would have another source-ness and chain.
His saying: (Not in need of a cause to bring it into existence) although it is in need of a cause for the attribution to it, which is the true simple, so its being derived from itself in consideration of its being subsequent to the effect.
His saying: (So that the source-nesses would form a chain) i.e., its chain comes to be.
His saying: (And if we grant its chaining) means that the granting does not refer to the Essence being a source for them, as is the initial understanding; because then it cannot be said that it is a chain in conceptual matters, but rather to what follows from it, namely the chain indicated by his saying: 'so that the source-nesses would form a chain', i.e., if we grant the occurrence of the chain of source-nesses by the intellect deriving from each source-ness another source-ness as a relation between it and the true simple, then this chain is in conceptual matters and it is not impossible because it is cut off according to the cessation of the intellect's consideration.
His saying: (For it is said, refining the mentioned proof so that the mentioned answer is repelled. His saying: (Particularity) does not mean the relational matter, otherwise what applies to source-ness would apply to it, but rather [it means] that the cause necessitates the existence of the effect in a particular manner. He did not say: There is no doubt that it exists; because it is the cause in reality, as in the exposition of Sharh al-Tajrid because there is no need for it; for then it would necessitate that the agent is not one... for the sake of [it is] real conceptual [matters] not merely hypothetical, and chaining in them is absolutely impossible. And it was answered: There is no chain, because they have no existence such that a cause for their existence is sought, nor does it follow that the attribution of the necessitating cause to them is a particular possible [matter] such that a cause for the attribution is sought, so on both estimates there is no need for another source-ness, and in it is what we indicated in the discussion of the increase of the existence of the Necessary.
His saying: (So chaining in conceptual matters is not impossible) contains a discussion; because source-ness, on the supposition that it needs another source-ness and chains, it could be said: The set of infinite source-nesses such that nothing escapes it needs another source-ness outside the set, so the totality is not all. The conclusion: even if the application of the proof of correspondence (برهان التطبيق) is not admitted here, it is impossible by another aspect.
His saying: (And that it must have a particularity) for you say: Why is it not permitted that the particularity belongs to the effect, such that the quiddity of the effect has a particularity with a specific cause that it does not have with another? ... (continues with the argument that without a special relation the effect would not be determined, leading to the conclusion that a single agent cannot have multiple effects without multiple aspects, and thus cannot be one from all aspects. The passage then discusses further objections and responses, including reference to al-Dawani's argument, and concludes that this judgment is nearly obvious but people overlook the meaning of unity.)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a sustained objection-and-answer argument. It begins with an objection: the cause must have a particularity (خصوصية) for each effect, otherwise it could not be the cause. The author then refines the definition of 'particularity' to mean the cause's necessitating the effect in a specific manner, not a relational matter. The objection of infinite regress is rejected because the particularity is a real conceptual matter, not a separate existing thing. The core argument then shows: if the agent is truly one (simple), it can have only one particularity. If multiple effects occur, the agent would need multiple particularities, which requires multiple aspects in its essence. Since a truly one agent has no aspects, it cannot have multiple particularities, so it cannot be the cause of multiple distinct effects. Therefore, the agent cannot be one from all aspects. The author notes that this is nearly obvious, but people overlook the meaning of unity. The passage continues to address further objections: that the particularity might belong to the effect, not the cause, and that the agent's simplicity might still be sufficient. The response reiterates that without multiple aspects, there cannot be multiple distinct particularities. The last part discusses what would follow if there were multiple aspects, leading to another infinite regress, and comments on al-Dawani's critique.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single simple agent can have multiple particularities without any change in its essence.
Correction: A simple agent has only one essence; having multiple particularities would require multiple aspects, which would negate its simplicity and true oneness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot a single simple agent produce two distinct effects?
A) Because it would need two particularities, but a simple essence can have only one
B) Because the effects would be identical
C) Because the agent would need to be in two places
D) Because the effects would cancel each other

Key Takeaway
A truly simple agent can only have one particularity and thus can only produce one effect; multiple effects necessitate multiple aspects and compromise true oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's attribute of 'Samadiyyah' (the Self-Sufficient, upon whom all depend), do you truly believe that He needs nothing and that all of creation stems from His single essence without any division? How does this lesson challenge any subconscious notion of God having 'parts'? What will you do today to correct that in your heart?
?
If a truly simple being can only produce one effect, then how can you trust that the diverse blessings in your life—health, wealth, family—all come from the same single source? Are you prone to attributing different aspects of your life to different 'causes' (e.g., luck, effort, people)? How will you remind yourself daily that Allah alone is the cause of everything?
?
The proof shows that multiplicity in effects requires multiplicity in the cause. Look at your own soul: are you striving for unity of purpose in serving Allah, or are you pulled in many directions by conflicting desires? What one thing must you eliminate from your life to make your intention purely single for Allah?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
