Three Types of Non-Finiteness for Physical Powers
Al-Ījī defines the three types of non-finiteness for physical powers and states the reason for confinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways in which a physical power can be considered non-finite according to philosophers: duration, intensity, and number, and why these are the only possibilities.

Core Idea
Non-finiteness of physical powers comes in only three forms: duration, intensity, and number.

In this passage, Al-Ījī presents the philosophers' doctrine concerning the limitations of physical powers. He identifies three respects in which a physical power cannot be infinite: its ability to act over infinite time, its ability to produce infinitely fast motion, and its ability to produce an infinite number of acts. He then explains why these three are exhaustive, grounding them in the nature of quantity. This lesson unpacks these definitions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the three negations: 'la fi al-mudda', 'la fi al-shidda', 'la fi al-ʿidda'.
The passage is from the 'Fifth Observatory' on physical powers.
2
Identify the three types of non-finiteness
The philosophers deny that a physical power can be infinite in three aspects: duration (infinite time), intensity (infinite speed), and number (infinite count). Write down each definition as given.
Intensity here means the speed of motion.
3
Understand the confinement argument
The passage states that these three are the only possibilities because finitude and non-finiteness are primary accidents of quantity. Reflect on why quantity is linked to these three: extension (duration), divisibility (intensity), and multitude (number).
This is a metaphysical claim about the nature of quantity.
4
Apply the concepts
Consider examples: Can a physical power produce an infinite number of acts in a finite time? According to this doctrine, no. Test your understanding by explaining why each type is impossible for physical powers.
Think about the limitations of created powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية لا في المدة ) أي لا تقوى أن تفعل في زمان غير متناه، سواء كان الفعل الصادر عنها واحداً أو متعدداً، ولا في الشدة ) أي لا تقوى أن تفعل حركة لا تكون حركة أخرى أسرع منها، ولا في العدة أي لا تقوى على فعل عدده غير متناه، سواء كان زمانه متناهياً أو غير متناه، وإنما انحصر لا تناهي القوى بحسب آثارها في هذه الأمور الثلاثة؛ لأن التناهي واللاتناهي بمعنى عدم الملكة من الأعراض الذاتية الأولية للكمية.
Fifth observatory - Fifth objective: The statement of the philosophers concerning physical power. [It is] not in duration, i.e., it is not able to act in an infinite time, whether the act emanating from it is one or multiple; and not in intensity, i.e., it is not able to perform a motion such that no other motion is faster than it; and not in number, i.e., it is not able to perform an act whose number is infinite, whether its time is finite or infinite. And the non-finiteness of powers with respect to their effects is confined to these three matters because finitude and non-finiteness in the sense of absence of capacity (ʿadam al-malaka) are among the primary essential accidents of quantity.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines the three ways in which a physical power can be considered non-finite (lātanāhī) in its effects: in duration (mudda), in intensity (shidda), and in number (ʿidda). The author first states the definition: 'not in duration' means the power cannot act over an infinite period of time, regardless of whether the act is a single act or multiple acts. 'Not in intensity' means it cannot perform a motion such that no other motion could be faster—essentially, it cannot have infinite speed. 'Not in number' means it cannot produce an infinite number of acts, whether those acts occur in a finite time or an infinite time. The author then explains why these three are the only possibilities: because finitude (tanāhī) and non-finiteness (lātanāhī) in the sense of 'absence of capacity' (ʿadam al-malaka) are among the primary essential accidents of quantity (al-kammiyya). Quantity is divisible, and its attributes of being finite or infinite apply primarily in terms of extension (duration), divisibility (intensity as degree), and multitude (number). Thus the philosopher's claim that physical power is not infinite in these three ways is a standard Aristotelian position.

Key Terms
القوة الجسمانية
المدة
الشدة
العدة
التناهي
اللاتناهي
عدم الملكة
الكمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-finiteness in intensity implies the power can produce an infinitely fast motion.
Correction: It implies the opposite: the power is not capable of producing motion faster than all others, i.e., infinite speed is impossible.

Checkpoint
What are the three aspects in which a physical power's non-finiteness is considered according to the philosophers?
A) Duration, intensity, number
B) Time, speed, quantity
C) Power, act, effect
D) Motion, rest, change

Key Takeaway
Physical powers are limited in three ways: they cannot act over infinite time, cannot produce infinite speed, and cannot produce an infinite number of acts, because finitude and non-finiteness are attributes of quantity.

Why Intensity Applies Only to Motion
Explains why intensity is specific to motion and temporal things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-finiteness in intensity (shidda) is specifically attributed to motion and temporal phenomena, and why it cannot apply to non-temporal acts.

Core Idea
Infinite intensity in a power would require an instantaneous act, impossible for temporal beings.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a subtle point in the text: why does the author mention 'motion' specifically instead of a more general term like 'act'? The commentary explains that non-finiteness in intensity is unique to motion and other temporal processes. This lesson unpacks that distinction, drawing on Ibn Sina's al-Shifā' and clarifying key terms like 'adam al-malaka versus salb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice how the commentator starts by explaining why 'motion' is specified.
Pay attention to the quote from al-Shifā'.
2
Identify the key definitions
Look for the definition of non-finiteness in intensity and the distinction between 'adam al-malaka and salb.
This is crucial for understanding the scope.
3
Connect to the proof
Understand why instantaneous action is impossible for temporal beings, and how this ties to the confinement to motion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this say about physical powers? They cannot be infinite in intensity.

بحسب الزيادة لكنه بالانقسامات غير متناهية؛ لانتفاء الجزء، فإذا اعتبر لاتناهيه بحسب الانتقاص، فهو لاتناهيه بحسب الشدة وفيه بحث لأن معنى اللاتناهي في الشدة كما تقوى على فعل حركة لا يمكن أسرع منها وهذا على نفي اللاتناهي في بأنه إن لم يكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهابة الشدة، وإن أمكن الأشد منه فلم يكن غير متناه الشدة فاسد؛ لأنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يمكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهاية الشدة، بل لا النقصان يوجب قوله : (إما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان إلخ) حاصله : أن يعتبر انتقاص الزمان بالانفصال مرات غير متناهية، وهذا الوجه وإن كان راجعاً إلى عدم التناهي بحسب العدة في مراتب الانفصال، لكن يعرض باعتباره للقوى التناهي واللاتناهي بحسب الشدة، وكذلك في حاشية التجريد . قوله : (ظاهر) (البطلان ) نقل عن الشارح أنه إشارة إلى وجه عدم تعرض المصنف له، وفيه تأمل؛ لأن المصنف سيجوز في بحث الخلاء كون الزمان في القصر بحيث لا يمكن أن يقع في جزئه حركة محققة فلا يجري فيه وجه الإبطال الذي ذكره الشارح، وإن كان الشارح يريد زعم المصنف هناك، فالظاهر أن مراد الشارح بيان ظهور البطلان عندهم لا على زعم المصنف فتأمل .
His saying: '(That it performs a motion, etc.)' — He specified motion although the appropriate [expression] for the preceding and following would be to say 'that it performs an act,' indicating that non-finiteness in intensity is specific to motion and what is analogous to it among temporal things, and the subsequent explanation indicates it; because what is entailed by the non-finiteness of power in intensity is that the act occurs in an instant (ān), and its impossibility is in temporal things. The Shaykh said in al-Shifā': 'We consider in this chapter examples of spatial motions which necessitate traversing some distance and differ in speed and slowness, and they are only possible in time, since distance cannot be traversed except in time — otherwise we would not divide the instant in correspondence with division of distance — and similarly what runs in analogy to spatial motions in which there is no speed or slowness, due to the necessity of its needing time; if something can occur in an instant and also in time, it is not our concern here.' His saying: '(Whether its time, etc.)' — There is a general and specific relationship between non-finiteness in duration and non-finiteness in number from one aspect. His saying: '(Non-finiteness of powers)' — The apparent [reading] is 'non-finiteness of the power.' His saying: '(In the sense of absence of capacity [ʿadam al-malaka])' — This is unlike non-finiteness in the sense of privation (salb), for it is not specific to quantity but the abstract entities are also described by it. His saying: '(i.e., it is not able to perform a motion such that no other motion is faster than it)' — This interpretation, and likewise the proof adduced for this claim, indicates that the claim is that it is not permissible for the physical power to be infinite in intensity in motion, and it does not indicate the negation of the permissibility of being infinite in intensity with respect to another act. Similarly, the argument he mentioned on the impossibility of non-finiteness with respect to duration and number is only concerning the particularity of motion. Then, when powers are described by non-finiteness with respect to their effect, one must consider either the number of effects — and that is non-finiteness with respect to number — or their time, and then either consider the non-finiteness of time in increase and multiplicity — which is non-finiteness with respect to duration — or consider its non-finiteness in decrease and paucity due to its admitting divisions that do not stop at a limit, which is non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. Then, non-finiteness in intensity is apparently false because powers differ in intensity like archers who shoot [arrows covering] distances and years. His saying: '(One consider either the number of effects) — disregarding the unity and multiplicity of time.' His saying: '(And [consider] their time) — i.e., disregarding their unity and multiplicity.' His saying: '(In increase) — by considering the description of time in itself.' His saying: '(And multiplicity) — by considering the accidental occurrence of number upon it by its division into hours, days, and months — that not that.' His saying: '(And [if one] consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Means that the time of the effect, although finite in increase, yet by divisions is infinite due to the absence of a part; if one considers its non-finiteness with respect to diminution, it is non-finiteness with respect to intensity. But there is investigation: Because the meaning of non-finiteness in intensity is like [the power] being able to perform a motion that cannot be faster, and this is only conceivable if the effect occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, as the commentator (quddisa sirruh) stated in his footnotes on al-Tajrīd, where he said: 'If that act occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, then the power is infinite in intensity; otherwise it is finite; and the shorter the time, the greater the intensity of the power.' So finiteness of time entails finiteness of power in intensity, and its non-finiteness in decrease entails its finiteness in intensity because then after every rank of its ranks there exists another rank more intense than it. The answer: That what is intended is that its non-finiteness in decrease due to possible divisions, when they go from potentiality to actuality and no division can occur after them, is the non-finiteness of the power in intensity. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the argument of the Shaykh in al-Najāt against non-finiteness in [intensity] — that if there is no effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity, and if a more intense is possible, then it is not infinite in intensity — is flawed; because we do not grant that if it cannot have an effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity. Rather, non-decrease entails... His saying: '(Either one consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Its summary is that one considers the reduction of time by division an infinite number of times, and this aspect, although it goes back to non-finiteness with respect to number in the ranks of division, yet the consideration of it brings about finiteness and non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. And similarly in the gloss on al-Tajrīd. His saying: '(Apparent) (falseness)' — It is transmitted that the commentator [said] that it is an indication to the aspect of the author's not addressing it, and there is reflection [on this]; because the author will permit in the discussion of void (khalā') that time can be so short that no verifiable motion can occur in its part, so the aspect of refutation mentioned by the commentator does not apply there. And if the commentator intends the author's claim there, then apparently the commentator's intent is to explain the apparentness of its falsity in their view, not based on the author's claim. So reflect.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This long passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of non-finiteness in intensity (shidda) and how it relates to the other two types. It opens with a commentary on the phrase 'that it performs a motion' emphasizing that intensity pertains specifically to motion and other temporal processes, because if a power were infinite in intensity, it would produce an effect in an instant (ān), which is impossible for temporal beings. The commentator cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifā' to clarify that motions are spatial and require time, and that infinite intensity would mean instantaneous action, which is not our concern. The text then clarifies the relationship between the three types: there is a 'general and specific' (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh) between duration and number. Next, it explains that 'lātanāhī' here means 'absence of capacity' (ʿadam al-malaka), distinguishing it from privation (salb) which applies to abstract entities. The interpretation of intensity as 'not able to perform a motion that no other motion is faster' is scrutinized: the proof shows that the claim is only about motion, not about other acts. The commentator then provides a classification: when considering non-finiteness of effect, one looks either at the number of effects (number) or at time. Time is then subdivided into increase/multiplicity (duration) and decrease/paucity due to divisible infinity (intensity). Then the commentator states that non-finiteness in intensity is apparently false, citing an analogy of archers' varying strength. The subsequent commentary on the gloss discusses the meaning of considering non-finiteness in decrease and its relation to intensity. A key objection and answer follows: some argue that if the time of the effect is non-finite in decrease, that would entail infinite intensity, but the answer is that the non-finiteness in decrease refers to the potential divisions possible, not an actual infinite. The commentator refutes Ibn Sina's argument in al-Najāt by denying that if no more intense effect is possible, it is the limit of intensity. Finally, there is a critical note about the author's possible position on void and whether the commentator's refutation holds there. This passage shows the depth of analysis in verifying the impossibility of infinite intensity for physical powers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite intensity could apply to any type of act, not just motion.
Correction: The commentary stresses that the impossibility of infinite intensity is specific to temporal processes like motion; non-temporal acts could potentially be considered differently.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is non-finiteness in intensity specifically attributed to motion?
A) Because motion is the only type of act that can be fast or slow
B) Because infinite intensity would require an instantaneous act, which is impossible for temporal beings
C) Because the author only discusses motion in this chapter
D) Because non-finiteness in intensity is defined in terms of speed

Key Takeaway
Non-finiteness in intensity is impossible for physical powers because it would necessitate an instantaneous act, which cannot occur in temporal beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your power cannot be infinite in intensity because you are a temporal being. In what areas of your life have you been demanding immediate, perfect results from yourself—expecting to change overnight? Where has this impatience actually hindered your growth? Commit today to embrace the slow, temporal process of gradual improvement, trusting that Allah rewards persistence, not just intensity.
?
The passage distinguishes between non-finiteness in intensity (one-time burst) and non-finiteness in duration (long-term consistency). Which of these two do you rely on more in your worship? Are you the type who does intense short-term acts but neglects consistent, small, daily deeds? What is one specific change you will make this week to shift your focus from intensity to duration in your relationship with Allah?
?
Ibn Sina says motion requires time because distance cannot be traversed in an instant. Reflect: Are you treating your spiritual journey like a distance that can be covered in one leap? In what specific aspect of your deen have you been expecting instant gratification (e.g., immediate taqwa, instant khushu')? How does accepting your created temporality change the way you approach that aspect from today onward?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Three Types of Non-Finiteness: Number, Duration, Intensity
Classifies non-finiteness into three types and states intensity is apparently false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of non-finiteness into three types: number (ʿidda), duration (mudda), and intensity (shidda), and how intensity is defined in terms of decrease and division.

Core Idea
Non-finiteness of power is categorized by effect count, time's increase, or time's decrease via division.

After establishing the domain of intensity, the commentator presents a systematic classification of the three ways a power can be considered non-finite: by the number of its effects, by the duration of its time, or by the intensity derived from the infinite divisibility of time. This lesson lays out that taxonomy and presents the initial claim that intensity is apparently false.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the passage that lists the three types. Notice the careful wording: number of effects, time in increase/duration, time in decrease/intensity.
2
Understand the basis of each type
For each type, ask: what is being considered? For number: effects disregarding time. For duration: time's increase. For intensity: time's decrease due to divisions.
3
Note the example for intensity
The commentator gives an analogy of archers to show that intensity is apparently false. Try to see why this analogy works.
4
Reflect on the relationship
Consider how the three types relate to each other. Does intensity reduce to number in some way? The commentary later addresses this.

قوله : ( أن تفعل حركة إلخ خص الحركة بالذكر مع أن المناسب للسابق واللاحق أن يقول : أن تفعل فعلاً إشارة إلى أن عدم التناهي في الشدة مختص بالحركة، وما يجري مجراها من الزمانيات، ويدل عليه البيان الآتي؛ لأن اللازم من عدم تناهي القوة في الشدة وقوع الفعل منها في آن، واستحالته إنما هو في الزمانيات. قال الشيخ في الشفاء : إنا نعتبر في هذا الباب أمثال الحركات المكانية التي توجب قطع مسافة ما وتختلف فيها بالسرعة والبطء ولا يمكن إلا في زمان إذ لا يمكن قطع المسافة إلا في آن وإلا لا نقسم الآن بإزاء انقسام المسافة، وكذلك ما يجري مجرى الحركات المكانية مما لم يقع فيه سرعة وبطء لضرورة حاجة ذلك إلى زمان، فإن كان شيء يحتمل أن يقع في الآن، وأن يقع في زمان فليس كلامنا فيه . قوله : ( سواء كان زمانه إلخ فبين عدم التناهي في المدة وعدم التناهي في العدة عموم و خصوص من وجه قوله : ( لا تناهي القوى الظاهر لا تناهي القوة. قوله: (بمعنى عدم الملكة بخلاف اللاتناهي بمعنى السلب، فإنه ليس مختصاً بالكم بل يتصف به المجردات أيضاً. قوله : ( أي لا تقوى أن تفعل حركة لا تكون حركة أخرى أسرع منها هذا التفسير، وكذا الدليل الذي أقيم على هذا المدعى يدل على أن المدعى عدم جواز كون القوة الجسمانية غير متناهية في الشدة في الحركة، ولا يدل على نفي جواز عدم التناهي بالشدة بحسب فعل آخر، وكذلك الاحتجاج الذي ذكره على امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب المدة والعدة إنما هو في خصوصية الحركة . فإذا وصف القوى باللاتناهي نظراً إلى أثرها، فلا بد أن يعتبر إما عدد الآثار، وذلك هو اللاتناهي بحسب العدة، وإما زمانها وحينئذ إما أن يعتبر لاتناهي الزمان في الزيادة والكثرة، وهو اللاتناهي بحسب المدة وإما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان والقلة بسبب قبوله للانقسامات التي لا تقف عند حد، فهو لاتناهي القوى بحسب الشدة. ثم إن اللاتناهي في الشدة ظاهر البطلان لأن القوى إذا اختلفت في الشدة كرماة تقطع والأعوام . قوله : ( أن يعتبر إما عدد الآثار مع قطع النظر عن وحدة الزمان وكثرته . قوله : ( وأما زمانها أي مع قطع النظر عن وحدتها وكثرتها . قوله : ( في الزيادة بأن يعتبر اتصاف الزمان في نفسه. قوله: (والكثرة) بأن يعتبر عروض العدد له بانقسامه إلى الساعات والأيام والشهور أن لا أن قوله : ( وإما أن يعتبر لا تناهيه في النقصان إلخ) يعني أن زمان الأثر، وإن كان متناهياً بحسب الزيادة لكنه بالانقسامات غير متناهية؛ لانتفاء الجزء، فإذا اعتبر لاتناهيه بحسب الانتقاص، فهو لاتناهيه بحسب الشدة وفيه بحث لأن معنى اللاتناهي في الشدة كما تقوى على فعل حركة لا يمكن أسرع منها وهذا إنما يتصور إذا وقع الأثر في زمان في غاية القصر، بل في آن على ما صرح به الشارح قدس سره في حواشي التجريد، حيث قال: فإن وقع ذلك الفعل في زمان في غاية القصر، بل في آن كانت القوة غير متناهية في الشدة، وإلا والجواب : أن المراد أن لا تناهيه في النقصان بسبب الانقسامات الممكنة إذا خرجت من القوة إلى الفعل ولا يمكن بعدها انقسام أصلا هو لا تناهي الشدة القوة بحسب الشدة، وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن استدلال الشيخ في النجاة على نفي اللاتناهي في بأنه إن لم يكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهابة الشدة، وإن أمكن الأشد منه فلم يكن غير متناه الشدة فاسد؛ لأنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يمكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهاية الشدة، بل لا النقصان يوجب قوله : (إما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان إلخ) حاصله : أن يعتبر انتقاص الزمان بالانفصال مرات غير متناهية، وهذا الوجه وإن كان راجعاً إلى عدم التناهي بحسب العدة في مراتب الانفصال، لكن يعرض باعتباره للقوى التناهي واللاتناهي بحسب الشدة، وكذلك في حاشية التجريد . قوله : (ظاهر) (البطلان ) نقل عن الشارح أنه إشارة إلى وجه عدم تعرض المصنف له، وفيه تأمل؛ لأن المصنف سيجوز في بحث الخلاء كون الزمان في القصر بحيث لا يمكن أن يقع في جزئه حركة محققة فلا يجري فيه وجه الإبطال الذي ذكره الشارح، وإن كان الشارح يريد زعم المصنف هناك، فالظاهر أن مراد الشارح بيان ظهور البطلان عندهم لا على زعم المصنف فتأمل .
His saying: '(That it performs a motion, etc.)' — He specified motion although the appropriate [expression] for the preceding and following would be to say 'that it performs an act,' indicating that non-finiteness in intensity is specific to motion and what is analogous to it among temporal things, and the subsequent explanation indicates it; because what is entailed by the non-finiteness of power in intensity is that the act occurs in an instant (ān), and its impossibility is in temporal things. The Shaykh said in al-Shifā': 'We consider in this chapter examples of spatial motions which necessitate traversing some distance and differ in speed and slowness, and they are only possible in time, since distance cannot be traversed except in time — otherwise we would not divide the instant in correspondence with division of distance — and similarly what runs in analogy to spatial motions in which there is no speed or slowness, due to the necessity of its needing time; if something can occur in an instant and also in time, it is not our concern here.' His saying: '(Whether its time, etc.)' — There is a general and specific relationship between non-finiteness in duration and non-finiteness in number from one aspect. His saying: '(Non-finiteness of powers)' — The apparent [reading] is 'non-finiteness of the power.' His saying: '(In the sense of absence of capacity [ʿadam al-malaka])' — This is unlike non-finiteness in the sense of privation (salb), for it is not specific to quantity but the abstract entities are also described by it. His saying: '(i.e., it is not able to perform a motion such that no other motion is faster than it)' — This interpretation, and likewise the proof adduced for this claim, indicates that the claim is that it is not permissible for the physical power to be infinite in intensity in motion, and it does not indicate the negation of the permissibility of being infinite in intensity with respect to another act. Similarly, the argument he mentioned on the impossibility of non-finiteness with respect to duration and number is only concerning the particularity of motion. Then, when powers are described by non-finiteness with respect to their effect, one must consider either the number of effects — and that is non-finiteness with respect to number — or their time, and then either consider the non-finiteness of time in increase and multiplicity — which is non-finiteness with respect to duration — or consider its non-finiteness in decrease and paucity due to its admitting divisions that do not stop at a limit, which is non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. Then, non-finiteness in intensity is apparently false because powers differ in intensity like archers who shoot [arrows covering] distances and years. His saying: '(One consider either the number of effects) — disregarding the unity and multiplicity of time.' His saying: '(And [consider] their time) — i.e., disregarding their unity and multiplicity.' His saying: '(In increase) — by considering the description of time in itself.' His saying: '(And multiplicity) — by considering the accidental occurrence of number upon it by its division into hours, days, and months — that not that.' His saying: '(And [if one] consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Means that the time of the effect, although finite in increase, yet by divisions is infinite due to the absence of a part; if one considers its non-finiteness with respect to diminution, it is non-finiteness with respect to intensity. But there is investigation: Because the meaning of non-finiteness in intensity is like [the power] being able to perform a motion that cannot be faster, and this is only conceivable if the effect occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, as the commentator (quddisa sirruh) stated in his footnotes on al-Tajrīd, where he said: 'If that act occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, then the power is infinite in intensity; otherwise it is finite; and the shorter the time, the greater the intensity of the power.' So finiteness of time entails finiteness of power in intensity, and its non-finiteness in decrease entails its finiteness in intensity because then after every rank of its ranks there exists another rank more intense than it. The answer: That what is intended is that its non-finiteness in decrease due to possible divisions, when they go from potentiality to actuality and no division can occur after them, is the non-finiteness of the power in intensity. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the argument of the Shaykh in al-Najāt against non-finiteness in [intensity] — that if there is no effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity, and if a more intense is possible, then it is not infinite in intensity — is flawed; because we do not grant that if it cannot have an effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity. Rather, non-decrease entails... His saying: '(Either one consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Its summary is that one considers the reduction of time by division an infinite number of times, and this aspect, although it goes back to non-finiteness with respect to number in the ranks of division, yet the consideration of it brings about finiteness and non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. And similarly in the gloss on al-Tajrīd. His saying: '(Apparent) (falseness)' — It is transmitted that the commentator [said] that it is an indication to the aspect of the author's not addressing it, and there is reflection [on this]; because the author will permit in the discussion of void (khalā') that time can be so short that no verifiable motion can occur in its part, so the aspect of refutation mentioned by the commentator does not apply there. And if the commentator intends the author's claim there, then apparently the commentator's intent is to explain the apparentness of its falsity in their view, not based on the author's claim. So reflect.

Commentary
This long passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of non-finiteness in intensity (shidda) and how it relates to the other two types. Objections and Refinements on Infinite Intensity
Addresses objections about division and intensity, refutes Ibn Sina, and notes an exception about void.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that infinite divisibility of time might entail infinite intensity, the response that it refers to potential divisions, and the critique of Ibn Sina's argument in al-Najāt.

Core Idea
Potential infinite division does not imply actual infinite intensity; the objection is answered by distinguishing potential from actual.

The classification raised a question: if time can be divided infinitely, wouldn't that allow for infinite intensity? The commentator addresses this objection, citing glosses on al-Tajrīd, and then critiques Ibn Sina's argument from al-Najāt. Finally, a note on void (al-khalā') adds nuance. This lesson unpacks these advanced discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The objection: if time is non-finite in decrease, then after every rank there is a more intense rank, implying infinite intensity. Find how the commentator answers.
Look for the phrase 'والجواب'.
2
Study the critique of Ibn Sina
The commentator finds fault with Ibn Sina's argument in al-Najāt. Identify the flaw.
The key is that not being able to have a more intense effect does not necessarily mean it's the limit.
3
Consider the void exception
The passage ends with a note that in the discussion of void, time might be so short that no motion occurs, challenging the refutation. Reflect on this.
4
Synthesize
Combine the points: intensity is impossible for physical powers, but the reasoning involves subtle distinctions between potential and actual divisions.

أقيم على هذا المدعى يدل على أن المدعى عدم جواز كون القوة الجسمانية غير متناهية في الشدة في الحركة، ولا يدل على نفي جواز عدم التناهي بالشدة بحسب فعل آخر، وكذلك الاحتجاج الذي ذكره على امتناع اللاتناهي بحسب المدة والعدة إنما هو في خصوصية الحركة . فإذا وصف القوى باللاتناهي نظراً إلى أثرها، فلا بد أن يعتبر إما عدد الآثار، وذلك هو اللاتناهي بحسب العدة، وإما زمانها وحينئذ إما أن يعتبر لاتناهي الزمان في الزيادة والكثرة، وهو اللاتناهي بحسب المدة وإما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان والقلة بسبب قبوله للانقسامات التي لا تقف عند حد، فهو لاتناهي القوى بحسب الشدة. ثم إن اللاتناهي في الشدة ظاهر البطلان لأن القوى إذا اختلفت في الشدة كرماة تقطع والأعوام . قوله : ( أن يعتبر إما عدد الآثار مع قطع النظر عن وحدة الزمان وكثرته . قوله : ( وأما زمانها أي مع قطع النظر عن وحدتها وكثرتها . قوله : ( في الزيادة بأن يعتبر اتصاف الزمان في نفسه. قوله: (والكثرة) بأن يعتبر عروض العدد له بانقسامه إلى الساعات والأيام والشهور أن لا أن قوله : ( وإما أن يعتبر لا تناهيه في النقصان إلخ) يعني أن زمان الأثر، وإن كان متناهياً بحسب الزيادة لكنه بالانقسامات غير متناهية؛ لانتفاء الجزء، فإذا اعتبر لاتناهيه بحسب الانتقاص، فهو لاتناهيه بحسب الشدة وفيه بحث لأن معنى اللاتناهي في الشدة كما تقوى على فعل حركة لا يمكن أسرع منها وهذا إنما يتصور إذا وقع الأثر في زمان في غاية القصر، بل في آن على ما صرح به الشارح قدس سره في حواشي التجريد، حيث قال: فإن وقع ذلك الفعل في زمان في غاية القصر، بل في آن كانت القوة غير متناهية في الشدة، وإلا كانت متناهية، وكلما كان الزمان أقصر كانت القوة أشد فإذن تناهي الزمان في تناهي القوة في الشدة ولاتناهيه في النقصان يوجب تناهيها في الشدة لأنه حينئذ يوجد بعد كل مرتبة من مراتبها مرتبة أخرى أشد منها والجواب : أن المراد أن لا تناهيه في النقصان بسبب الانقسامات الممكنة إذا خرجت من القوة إلى الفعل ولا يمكن بعدها انقسام أصلا هو لا تناهي الشدة القوة بحسب الشدة، وبما ذكرنا ظهر أن استدلال الشيخ في النجاة على نفي اللاتناهي في بأنه إن لم يكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهابة الشدة، وإن أمكن الأشد منه فلم يكن غير متناه الشدة فاسد؛ لأنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يمكن أثراً لقوة أشد مما كان فهو نهاية الشدة، بل لا النقصان يوجب قوله : (إما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان إلخ) حاصله : أن يعتبر انتقاص الزمان بالانفصال مرات غير متناهية، وهذا الوجه وإن كان راجعاً إلى عدم التناهي بحسب العدة في مراتب الانفصال، لكن يعرض باعتباره للقوى التناهي واللاتناهي بحسب الشدة، وكذلك في حاشية التجريد . قوله : (ظاهر) (البطلان ) نقل عن الشارح أنه إشارة إلى وجه عدم تعرض المصنف له، وفيه تأمل؛ لأن المصنف سيجوز في بحث الخلاء كون الزمان في القصر بحيث لا يمكن أن يقع في جزئه حركة محققة فلا يجري فيه وجه الإبطال الذي ذكره الشارح، وإن كان الشارح يريد زعم المصنف هناك، فالظاهر أن مراد الشارح بيان ظهور البطلان عندهم لا على زعم المصنف فتأمل .
His saying: '(That it performs a motion, etc.)' — He specified motion although the appropriate [expression] for the preceding and following would be to say 'that it performs an act,' indicating that non-finiteness in intensity is specific to motion and what is analogous to it among temporal things, and the subsequent explanation indicates it; because what is entailed by the non-finiteness of power in intensity is that the act occurs in an instant (ān), and its impossibility is in temporal things. The Shaykh said in al-Shifā': 'We consider in this chapter examples of spatial motions which necessitate traversing some distance and differ in speed and slowness, and they are only possible in time, since distance cannot be traversed except in time — otherwise we would not divide the instant in correspondence with division of distance — and similarly what runs in analogy to spatial motions in which there is no speed or slowness, due to the necessity of its needing time; if something can occur in an instant and also in time, it is not our concern here.' His saying: '(Whether its time, etc.)' — There is a general and specific relationship between non-finiteness in duration and non-finiteness in number from one aspect. His saying: '(Non-finiteness of powers)' — The apparent [reading] is 'non-finiteness of the power.' His saying: '(In the sense of absence of capacity [ʿadam al-malaka])' — This is unlike non-finiteness in the sense of privation (salb), for it is not specific to quantity but the abstract entities are also described by it. His saying: '(i.e., it is not able to perform a motion such that no other motion is faster than it)' — This interpretation, and likewise the proof adduced for this claim, indicates that the claim is that it is not permissible for the physical power to be infinite in intensity in motion, and it does not indicate the negation of the permissibility of being infinite in intensity with respect to another act. Similarly, the argument he mentioned on the impossibility of non-finiteness with respect to duration and number is only concerning the particularity of motion. Then, when powers are described by non-finiteness with respect to their effect, one must consider either the number of effects — and that is non-finiteness with respect to number — or their time, and then either consider the non-finiteness of time in increase and multiplicity — which is non-finiteness with respect to duration — or consider its non-finiteness in decrease and paucity due to its admitting divisions that do not stop at a limit, which is non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. Then, non-finiteness in intensity is apparently false because powers differ in intensity like archers who shoot [arrows covering] distances and years. His saying: '(One consider either the number of effects) — disregarding the unity and multiplicity of time.' His saying: '(And [consider] their time) — i.e., disregarding their unity and multiplicity.' His saying: '(In increase) — by considering the description of time in itself.' His saying: '(And multiplicity) — by considering the accidental occurrence of number upon it by its division into hours, days, and months — that not that.' His saying: '(And [if one] consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Means that the time of the effect, although finite in increase, yet by divisions is infinite due to the absence of a part; if one considers its non-finiteness with respect to diminution, it is non-finiteness with respect to intensity. But there is investigation: Because the meaning of non-finiteness in intensity is like [the power] being able to perform a motion that cannot be faster, and this is only conceivable if the effect occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, as the commentator (quddisa sirruh) stated in his footnotes on al-Tajrīd, where he said: 'If that act occurs in an extremely short time, even in an instant, then the power is infinite in intensity; otherwise it is finite; and the shorter the time, the greater the intensity of the power.' So finiteness of time entails finiteness of power in intensity, and its non-finiteness in decrease entails its finiteness in intensity because then after every rank of its ranks there exists another rank more intense than it. The answer: That what is intended is that its non-finiteness in decrease due to possible divisions, when they go from potentiality to actuality and no division can occur after them, is the non-finiteness of the power in intensity. And by what we have mentioned, it is clear that the argument of the Shaykh in al-Najāt against non-finiteness in [intensity] — that if there is no effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity, and if a more intense is possible, then it is not infinite in intensity — is flawed; because we do not grant that if it cannot have an effect of a power more intense than it was, then it is the limit of intensity. Rather, non-decrease entails... His saying: '(Either one consider its non-finiteness in decrease, etc.)' — Its summary is that one considers the reduction of time by division an infinite number of times, and this aspect, although it goes back to non-finiteness with respect to number in the ranks of division, yet the consideration of it brings about finiteness and non-finiteness of powers with respect to intensity. And similarly in the gloss on al-Tajrīd. His saying: '(Apparent) (falseness)' — It is transmitted that the commentator [said] that it is an indication to the aspect of the author's not addressing it, and there is reflection [on this]; because the author will permit in the discussion of void (khalā') that time can be so short that no verifiable motion can occur in its part, so the aspect of refutation mentioned by the commentator does not apply there. And if the commentator intends the author's claim there, then apparently the commentator's intent is to explain the apparentness of its falsity in their view, not based on the author's claim. So reflect.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This long passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of non-finiteness in intensity (shidda) and how it relates to the other two types. It opens with a commentary on the phrase 'that it performs a motion' emphasizing that intensity pertains specifically to motion and other temporal processes, because if a power were infinite in intensity, it would produce an effect in an instant (ān), which is impossible for temporal beings. The commentator cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifā' to clarify that motions are spatial and require time, and that infinite intensity would mean instantaneous action, which is not our concern. The text then clarifies the relationship between the three types: there is a 'general and specific' (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh) between duration and number. Next, it explains that 'lātanāhī' here means 'absence of capacity' (ʿadam al-malaka), distinguishing it from privation (salb) which applies to abstract entities. The interpretation of intensity as 'not able to perform a motion that no other motion is faster' is scrutinized: the proof shows that the claim is only about motion, not about other acts. The commentator then provides a classification: when considering non-finiteness of effect, one looks either at the number of effects (number) or at time. Time is then subdivided into increase/multiplicity (duration) and decrease/paucity due to divisible infinity (intensity). Then the commentator states that non-finiteness in intensity is apparently false, citing an analogy of archers' varying strength. The subsequent commentary on the gloss discusses the meaning of considering non-finiteness in decrease and its relation to intensity. A key objection and answer follows: some argue that if the time of the effect is non-finite in decrease, that would entail infinite intensity, but the answer is that the non-finiteness in decrease refers to the potential divisions possible, not an actual infinite. The commentator refutes Ibn Sina's argument in al-Najāt by denying that if no more intense effect is possible, it is the limit of intensity. Finally, there is a critical note about the author's possible position on void and whether the commentator's refutation holds there. This passage shows the depth of analysis in verifying the impossibility of infinite intensity for physical powers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that infinite divisibility of time would logically lead to an actual infinite intensity of power.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the non-finiteness in decrease is potential; actual divisions are not realized, so no actual infinite intensity.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator answer the objection that non-finiteness in decrease would imply infinite intensity?
A) He denies that time can be infinitely divided
B) He says the non-finiteness refers to potential divisions, not actual
C) He accepts the objection and modifies the definition
D) He argues that intensity can be infinite in the case of void

Key Takeaway
Potential infinite division does not entail actual infinite intensity; the objection is resolved by distinguishing potentiality from actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a rational argument about your faith without asking whether it confuses what is possible in theory with what is real in practice? Where in your life are you treating abstract possibilities as certainties? How can you ground your beliefs in actual revelation rather than mere intellectual speculation?
?
The commentator shows that even a great thinker like Ibn Sina can err in his reasoning. Are there areas in your worship or conviction where you have blindly followed a scholar's argument without examining it against the Qur'an and Sunnah? Commit to scrutinizing one of your deeply held assumptions today, even if it feels uncomfortable.
?
This lesson distinguishes between potential and actual infinity. In your own spiritual journey, are you mistaking potential good intentions for actual righteous actions? What is one intention you have left unrealized that you will turn into a concrete deed this week?

Classification of Infinity in Bodily Power
Author enumerates three ways a bodily power can be considered infinite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of infinity that can be attributed to bodily power: number, duration, and intensity.

Core Idea
Bodily power can be infinite in three distinct ways: number of effects, duration, or intensity.

The author begins by systematically classifying the senses in which a bodily power may be described as infinite. This classification is essential because the later arguments depend on distinguishing these concepts. We will see that each type has different implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the author's systematic breakdown.
2
Identify the three types
Find each type: number of effects, duration, and intensity. Note how each is defined.
3
Reflect on why they are distinct
Consider why the author separates these three. How does each type differ in its relationship to time and effect?
This classification is foundational for the arguments that follow.

المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية. فإذا وصف القوى باللاتناهي نظراً إلى أثرها، فلا بد أن يعتبر إما عدد الآثار، وذلك هو اللاتناهي بحسب العدة، وإما زمانها وحينئذ إما أن يعتبر لاتناهي الزمان في الزيادة والكثرة، وهو اللاتناهي بحسب المدة وإما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان والقلة بسبب قبوله للانقسامات التي لا تقف عند حد، فهو لاتناهي القوى بحسب الشدة. ثم إن اللاتناهي في الشدة ظاهر البطلان لأن القوى إذا اختلفت في الشدة كرماة تقطع سهامهم مسافة واحدة محدودة في أزمنة مختلفة فلا شك أن التي زمانها أقل هي أشد قوة من التي زمانها أكثر ، فما تكون غير متناهية في الشدة وجب أن تقع الحركة الصادرة عنها لا في زمان؛ إذ لو وقعت في زمان وكل زمان قابل للقسمة فالحركة الواقعة في نصف ذلك الزمان مع اتحاد المسافة تكون أسرع، فمصدرها أشد وأقوى، فلا يكون مصدر الأولى غير متناه في الشدة، والمقدر خلافه، لكن وقوع الحركة لا في زمان، بل في آن محال؛ لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة، فتنقسم بانقسامها، ويكون مقدارها أعني الزمان منقسماً أيضاً. واعترض عليه بأنا لا نسلم أن قطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان ممكن في نفس الأمر، وإمكان فرض قطعها نفعاً، يجدي لجواز أن يكون المفروض محالاً مستلزماً لمحال آخر، وأما اللاتناهي أبداً في المدة أو العدة، فقد جوزه المتكلمون ؛ لأن نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار لا نهاية في الشدة؛ لما عرفت من أن المراد باللاتناهي في الشدة أن لا يمكن أثر أشد منه، وإن وصفه باللاتناهي باعتبار أنه لا يمكن تحققه إلا بعد حصول جميع الانقسامات الغير المتناهية وخروجها عن القوة إلى الفعل؛ لا أن الشدة لم تبلغ النهاية واعلم أن هذا البيان أعم مأخذاً من المدعى؛ لأنه يفيد امتناع وجود حركة هي أسرع الحركات، سواء صدرت من قوة جسمانية، أو مجردة. والتخصيص في المدعى بناء على أنه المقصود بالبيان . قوله : ( واعترض عليه إلخ ) أجاب عنه بعض المحققين بأن اللاتناهي في الشدة يقتضي أن لا يجوز العقل ما هو أشد منه فلم يكن غير متناه في الشدة لأن الزيادة على غير المتناهي المتسق النظام في الجانب الذي كان غير متناه تنافي اللاتناهي، وفيه أن تجويز العقل للأشد منه تجويزاً مطابقاً للواقع ممنوع ، والتجويز الفرضي لا يجدي نفعاً. قوله : ( فقد جوزه المتكلمون ) أي غير الأشاعرة القائلون بتأثير The Fifth Observatory - The Fifth Section: The Statement of the Philosophers concerning Bodily Power. When powers are described as infinite with respect to their effect, then it must be considered either the number of effects—that is infinity in terms of number (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-ʿidda)—or their time, and then either the infinity of time in increase and multiplicity, which is infinity in terms of duration (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-mudda), or its infinity in decrease and paucity due to its acceptance of divisions that do not stop at a limit, and that is the infinity of powers in terms of intensity (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-shidda). Then indeed infinity in intensity is evidently false, because if powers differ in intensity, like archers who shoot their arrows over one limited distance in different times, there is no doubt that the one whose time is less is stronger in power than the one whose time is more. So that which is infinite in intensity must have the motion proceeding from it occur not in time; for if it occurred in time, and every time is divisible, then the motion occurring in half of that time, the distance being the same, would be faster, so its source would be stronger and more powerful, and thus the source of the first would not be infinite in intensity—contrary to the supposition. But the occurrence of motion not in time, but in an instant (ān), is impossible, because every motion is over a divisible distance, so it is divided by its division, and its measure, i.e., time, is also divisible. And an objection was made: We do not concede that traversing that distance in half of that time is possible in actuality, and the possibility of assuming its traversal is of no use, because it may be that the assumed is impossible entailing another impossibility. As for infinity forever in duration or number, the theologians (mutakallimūn) have allowed it, because the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell have no end in intensity; because you have known that the meaning of infinity in intensity is that an effect stronger than it cannot exist, and it is described as infinite by virtue of it not being realizable except after the attainment of all infinite divisions and their emergence from potentiality to actuality, not that the intensity has not reached the limit. And know that this explanation is more general in premise than the claim; because it implies the impossibility of the existence of a motion that is the fastest of motions, whether it proceeds from a bodily power or from an immaterial one. The specification in the claim is based on it being the intended object of the explanation. His statement: 'and an objection was made, etc.'—some investigators answered it by saying that infinity in intensity requires that the intellect does not admit what is stronger than it, so it is not infinite in intensity, because increase upon the infinite that is consistent in order in the aspect that was infinite contradicts infinity. And in it [the answer] is that the intellect's admission of something stronger than it, if it is an admission corresponding to reality, is denied, and a hypothetical admission is of no benefit. His statement: 'the theologians have allowed it'—i.e., other than the Ashʿarīs who hold the influence of the powers that preserve the body. His statement: 'because every motion is over a divisible distance, etc.'—the intended meaning here is motion in the sense of traversal (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ), but motion in the sense of mediation (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-tawassuṭ) is instantaneous, and the body is not described by it in terms of intensity or lack of infinity therein, because intensity in motion is with respect to its speed, and its lack of infinity in intensity is with respect to there being no motion faster than it, as the commentator indicated; and speed and slowness are with respect to traversal of distance, and there is no traversal except by motion in the sense of traversal. Also, its infinity [i.e., of the motion in the sense of traversal] is with respect to time having reached through acceptance of infinite divisions to what this motion coincided with, as you know. And time never reaches an instant according to the philosophers. Then motion in the sense of traversal, although it is something imaginary, yet they apply to it the judgments of existence based on it being from existing things, i.e., motion in the sense of mediation, as will come, hence they consider it an effect of bodily power. His statement: 'and as for infinity in duration and number, the theologians have allowed it'—the Ashʿarīs who hold that it depends...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion from the commentary on al-Mawaqif concerning bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya) and how it can be described as infinite. The passage begins by classifying the ways in which a power may be considered infinite with respect to its effect. There are three ways: (1) infinity in number of effects (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-ʿidda), (2) infinity in time considered as duration (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-mudda) when time is taken in its own increase, and (3) infinity in time considered as decrease (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-nuqsān) due to the infinite divisibility of time, which leads to infinity in intensity (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-shidda). The author then states that infinity in intensity is obviously false, and provides a proof using the example of archers shooting arrows over the same distance in different times. If a power were infinite in intensity, the motion it produces would have to occur in no time, because if it occurred in any time, half that time would yield a faster motion from a stronger power, contradicting the assumption of infinite intensity. But motion cannot occur in an instant because motion is over a divisible distance and thus requires divisible time. An objection is then raised: it is not conceded that traversing the distance in half the time is actually possible; mere hypothetical possibility does not prove impossibility. The text then turns to the theologians' (mutakallimūn) allowance of infinity in duration and number, citing the eternal bliss and punishment in the afterlife. The meaning of infinity in intensity is clarified: it is not that the intensity has no limit, but that the effect cannot be realized until all infinite divisions have actualized. The argument is general, implying the impossibility of a fastest motion from any power, but it is specifically applied to bodily power. The commentator then provides additional explanations: the answer of some investigators that infinite intensity precludes the intellect from conceiving something stronger, but this is criticized because such intellectual admission is not necessarily correspondent to reality. Finally, the commentator distinguishes two meanings of motion—motion as traversal (qaṭʿ) which is temporally extended, and motion as mediation (tawassuṭ) which is instantaneous. The former is considered an effect of bodily power despite being imaginary, and the latter is not described in terms of intensity. The passage ends with a note on the Ashʿarī position regarding infinity in duration and number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinity always means endless number.
Correction: Here infinity is categorized into three distinct senses, including duration and intensity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a type of infinity in bodily power?
A) speed
B) intensity
C) force
D) mass

Key Takeaway
Bodily power can be infinite in three respects: the number of its effects, the duration of its effects, or the intensity of its effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given that bodily power can only be infinite in limited ways, how does this affect your understanding of your own physical limitations? Are you striving to use your finite bodily power for what truly matters in the sight of Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between infinite in number, duration, and intensity. Which of these types of 'infinity' do you most often wrongly attribute to yourself, and how does recognizing the true limit of your power humble you before the Creator?
?
If all bodily power is finite in these respects, then all human achievements are ultimately bounded. Does this realization cause you to reflect on the infinite power of Allah alone, and what practical change should this bring to your daily reliance on Him?

Proof of Impossibility of Infinite Intensity
Author proves impossibility of infinite intensity using archer analogy and addresses objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that bodily power cannot be infinite in intensity, including the archer example and the objection.

Core Idea
Infinite intensity would require motion in no time, but motion requires time—a logical impossibility.

Having defined infinite intensity, the author now argues that it is impossible. He uses a vivid example of archers shooting arrows over the same distance to demonstrate that an infinitely intense power would have to produce motion in an instant, which is impossible. An objection is then raised and addressed.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical steps.
2
Follow the reductio argument
Identify the assumption of infinite intensity, the archer example, and the conclusion that motion must occur in no time.
3
Understand the objection
Read the objection and consider why it challenges the proof's assumption about possibility.

لاتناهي الزمان في الزيادة والكثرة، وهو اللاتناهي بحسب المدة وإما أن يعتبر لاتناهيه في النقصان والقلة بسبب قبوله للانقسامات التي لا تقف عند حد، فهو لاتناهي القوى بحسب الشدة. ثم إن اللاتناهي في الشدة المتكلمون الأشاعرة القائلون باستناد
The Fifth Observatory - The Fifth Section: The Statement of the Philosophers concerning Bodily Power. When powers are described as infinite with respect to their effect, then it must be considered either the number of effects—that is infinity in terms of number (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-ʿidda)—or their time, and then either the infinity of time in increase and multiplicity, which is infinity in terms of duration (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-mudda), or its infinity in decrease and paucity due to its acceptance of divisions that do not stop at a limit, and that is the infinity of powers in terms of intensity (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-shidda). Then indeed infinity in intensity is evidently false, because if powers differ in intensity, like archers who shoot their arrows over one limited distance in different times, there is no doubt that the one whose time is less is stronger in power than the one whose time is more. So that which is infinite in intensity must have the motion proceeding from it occur not in time; for if it occurred in time, and every time is divisible, then the motion occurring in half of that time, the distance being the same, would be faster, so its source would be stronger and more powerful, and thus the source of the first would not be infinite in intensity—contrary to the supposition. But the occurrence of motion not in time, but in an instant (ān), is impossible, because every motion is over a divisible distance, so it is divided by its division, and its measure, i.e., time, is also divisible. And an objection was made: We do not concede that traversing that distance in half of that time is possible in actuality, and the possibility of assuming its traversal is of no use, because it may be that the assumed is impossible entailing another impossibility. As for infinity forever in duration or number, the theologians (mutakallimūn) have allowed it, because the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell have no end in intensity; because you have known that the meaning of infinity in intensity is that an effect stronger than it cannot exist, and it is described as infinite by virtue of it not being realizable except after the attainment of all infinite divisions and their emergence from potentiality to actuality, not that the intensity has not reached the limit. And know that this explanation is more general in premise than the claim; because it implies the impossibility of the existence of a motion that is the fastest of motions, whether it proceeds from a bodily power or from an immaterial one. The specification in the claim is based on it being the intended object of the explanation. His statement: 'and an objection was made, etc.'—some investigators answered it by saying that infinity in intensity requires that the intellect does not admit what is stronger than it, so it is not infinite in intensity, because increase upon the infinite that is consistent in order in the aspect that was infinite contradicts infinity. And in it [the answer] is that the intellect's admission of something stronger than it, if it is an admission corresponding to reality, is denied, and a hypothetical admission is of no benefit. His statement: 'the theologians have allowed it'—i.e., other than the Ashʿarīs who hold the influence of the powers that preserve the body. His statement: 'because every motion is over a divisible distance, etc.'—the intended meaning here is motion in the sense of traversal (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ), but motion in the sense of mediation (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-tawassuṭ) is instantaneous, and the body is not described by it in terms of intensity or lack of infinity therein, because intensity in motion is with respect to its speed, and its lack of infinity in intensity is with respect to there being no motion faster than it, as the commentator indicated; and speed and slowness are with respect to traversal of distance, and there is no traversal except by motion in the sense of traversal. Also, its infinity [i.e., of the motion in the sense of traversal] is with respect to time having reached through acceptance of infinite divisions to what this motion coincided with, as you know. And time never reaches an instant according to the philosophers. Then motion in the sense of traversal, although it is something imaginary, yet they apply to it the judgments of existence based on it being from existing things, i.e., motion in the sense of mediation, as will come, hence they consider it an effect of bodily power. His statement: 'and as for infinity in duration and number, the theologians have allowed it'—the Ashʿarīs who hold that it depends...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion from the commentary on al-Mawaqif concerning bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya) and how it can be described as infinite. The passage begins by classifying the ways in which a power may be considered infinite with respect to its effect. There are three ways: (1) infinity in number of effects (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-ʿidda), (2) infinity in time considered as duration (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-mudda) when time is taken in its own increase, and (3) infinity in time considered as decrease (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-nuqsān) due to the infinite divisibility of time, which leads to infinity in intensity (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-shidda). The author then states that infinity in intensity is obviously false, and provides a proof using the example of archers shooting arrows over the same distance in different times. If a power were infinite in intensity, the motion it produces would have to occur in no time, because if it occurred in any time, half that time would yield a faster motion from a stronger power, contradicting the assumption of infinite intensity. But motion cannot occur in an instant because motion is over a divisible distance and thus requires divisible time. An objection is then raised: it is not conceded that traversing the distance in half the time is actually possible; mere hypothetical possibility does not prove impossibility. The text then turns to the theologians' (mutakallimūn) allowance of infinity in duration and number, citing the eternal bliss and punishment in the afterlife. The meaning of infinity in intensity is clarified: it is not that the intensity has no limit, but that the effect cannot be realized until all infinite divisions have actualized. The argument is general, implying the impossibility of a fastest motion from any power, but it is specifically applied to bodily power. The commentator then provides additional explanations: the answer of some investigators that infinite intensity precludes the intellect from conceiving something stronger, but this is criticized because such intellectual admission is not necessarily correspondent to reality. Finally, the commentator distinguishes two meanings of motion—motion as traversal (qaṭʿ) which is temporally extended, and motion as mediation (tawassuṭ) which is instantaneous. The former is considered an effect of bodily power despite being imaginary, and the latter is not described in terms of intensity. The passage ends with a note on the Ashʿarī position regarding infinity in duration and number.

Checkpoint
In the proof, what would have to be true if a power were infinite in intensity?
A) It would produce motion in infinite time
B) It would produce motion in no time
C) It would produce motion at constant speed
D) It would produce no motion

Key Takeaway
Infinite intensity in bodily power is impossible because it would require motion to occur in no time, contradicting the nature of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own strength and abilities. Everything you do takes time—learning, working, even thinking. How does the impossibility of infinite intensity in bodily power humble you before the Creator, who acts without time? What does this mean for your daily reliance on Him?
?
The archer analogy shows that even the strongest archer still takes time. In what areas of your life do you act as if you can achieve things instantly, without patience or gradual effort? How can you realign your expectations with this truth?
?
If no created power can produce motion in an instant, then every good deed you do takes time and effort. Are you willing to invest that time, knowing that only Allah’s command is instantaneous? What will you start doing today that requires patience and continuous effort for His sake?

Infinity in Duration and Number: Theologians' View and Motion Clarified
Author explains allowance of infinity in duration/number and clarifies motion types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians allow infinity in duration and number, and how the distinction between two meanings of motion resolves potential objections.

Core Idea
Theologians accept infinite duration and number (e.g., afterlife bliss/punishment), but only when understood correctly through motion types.

The author now turns to the other two types of infinity: duration and number. Unlike intensity, these are considered possible by theologians. The discussion also clarifies a crucial distinction between motion as traversal (temporally extended) and motion as mediation (instantaneous), which is needed to avoid confusion about infinity in intensity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on theologians' allowance
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the reference to the afterlife as an example.
2
Understand the clarification of infinite intensity
The author explains that infinite intensity does not mean no limit, but rather that the effect requires actualization of all infinite divisions.
3
Study the distinction of motion types
Identify the two meanings of motion: traversal (qaṭʿ) and mediation (tawassuṭ). Understand why only traversal is relevant for intensity and infinity.
This distinction resolves how motion can be an effect of bodily power.

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion from the commentary on al-Mawaqif concerning bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismāniyya) and how it can be described as infinite. The passage begins by classifying the ways in which a power may be considered infinite with respect to its effect. There are three ways: (1) infinity in number of effects (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-ʿidda), (2) infinity in time considered as duration (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-mudda) when time is taken in its own increase, and (3) infinity in time considered as decrease (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-nuqsān) due to the infinite divisibility of time, which leads to infinity in intensity (al-lātanāhī bi-ḥasab al-shidda). The author then states that infinity in intensity is obviously false, and provides a proof using the example of archers shooting arrows over the same distance in different times. If a power were infinite in intensity, the motion it produces would have to occur in no time, because if it occurred in any time, half that time would yield a faster motion from a stronger power, contradicting the assumption of infinite intensity. But motion cannot occur in an instant because motion is over a divisible distance and thus requires divisible time. An objection is then raised: it is not conceded that traversing the distance in half the time is actually possible; mere hypothetical possibility does not prove impossibility. The text then turns to the theologians' (mutakallimūn) allowance of infinity in duration and number, citing the eternal bliss and punishment in the afterlife. The meaning of infinity in intensity is clarified: it is not that the intensity has no limit, but that the effect cannot be realized until all infinite divisions have actualized. The argument is general, implying the impossibility of a fastest motion from any power, but it is specifically applied to bodily power. The commentator then provides additional explanations: the answer of some investigators that infinite intensity precludes the intellect from conceiving something stronger, but this is criticized because such intellectual admission is not necessarily correspondent to reality. Finally, the commentator distinguishes two meanings of motion—motion as traversal (qaṭʿ) which is temporally extended, and motion as mediation (tawassuṭ) which is instantaneous. The former is considered an effect of bodily power despite being imaginary, and the latter is not described in terms of intensity. The passage ends with a note on the Ashʿarī position regarding infinity in duration and number.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theologians deny all forms of infinity in bodily power.
Correction: They allow infinity in duration and number, only denying infinity in intensity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

The Philosophers' Argument on Infinite Intensity
Philosophers' argument deducing impossibility of infinite intensity from motion's relation to time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' deductive argument that a force infinite in intensity would necessitate motion in no time, which is impossible, thus rejecting the existence of infinite physical power.

Core Idea
Infinite intensity would break the necessary link between motion and time.

This lesson presents a classic argument from the philosophers (falasifa) against the possibility of a force being infinite in intensity. They reason that if a force were infinite, the motion it produces would occur in zero time, but all motion requires time and divisibility. This sets the stage for our study of causality and the nature of physical powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style and logical flow.
2
Identify the core premise
The argument begins with a comparison: a force covering a fixed distance in less time is stronger. Understand this relational premise.
3
Follow the deduction to impossibility
Trace how the premise leads to the conclusion that infinite intensity would require motion in no time, which is absurd because motion inherently involves divisible time and distance.
This is the heart of the philosophers' refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية

سهامهم [؟] مسافة واحدة محدودة في أزمنة مختلفة فلا شك أن التي زمانها أقل هي أشد قوة من التي زمانها أكثر ، فما تكون غير متناهية في الشدة وجب أن تقع الحركة الصادرة عنها لا في زمان؛ إذ لو وقعت في زمان وكل زمان قابل للقسمة فالحركة الواقعة في نصف ذلك الزمان مع اتحاد المسافة تكون أسرع، فمصدرها أشد وأقوى، فلا يكون مصدر الأولى غير متناه في الشدة، والمقدر خلافه، لكن وقوع الحركة لا في زمان، بل في آن محال؛ لأن كل حركة إنما هي على مسافة منقسمة، فتنقسم بانقسامها، ويكون مقدارها أعني الزمان منقسماً أيضاً. واعترض عليه بأنا لا نسلم أن قطع تلك المسافة في نصف ذلك الزمان ممكن في نفس الأمر، وإمكان فرض قطعها نفعاً، يجدي لجواز أن يكون المفروض محالاً مستلزماً لمحال آخر، وأما اللاتناهي أبداً في المدة أو العدة، فقد جوزه المتكلمون ؛ لأن نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار لا نهاية في الشدة؛ لما عرفت من أن المراد باللاتناهي في الشدة أن لا يمكن أثر أشد منه، وإن وصفه باللاتناهي باعتبار أنه لا يمكن تحققه إلا بعد حصول جميع الانقسامات الغير المتناهية وخروجها عن القوة إلى الفعل؛ لا أن الشدة لم تبلغ النهاية واعلم أن هذا البيان أعم مأخذاً من المدعى؛ لأنه يفيد امتناع وجود حركة هي أسرع الحركات، سواء صدرت من قوة جسمانية، أو مجردة. والتخصيص في المدعى بناء على أنه المقصود بالبيان .

قوله : ( واعترض عليه إلخ ) أجاب عنه بعض المحققين بأن اللاتناهي في الشدة يقتضي أن لا يجوز العقل ما هو أشد منه فلم يكن غير متناه في الشدة لأن الزيادة على غير المتناهي المتسق النظام في الجانب الذي كان غير متناه تنافي اللاتناهي، وفيه أن تجويز العقل للأشد منه تجويزاً مطابقاً للواقع ممنوع ، والتجويز الفرضي لا يجدي نفعاً.
The Fifth Observation - The Fifth Aim: The Statement of the Philosophers on Physical Force.

As for their arrows [؟] a single limited distance in different times, there is no doubt that the one whose time is less is stronger in force than the one whose time is more. So if it is infinite in intensity, it must be that the motion proceeding from it occurs not in time; for if it occurred in time, and every time is divisible, then the motion occurring in half of that time while the distance is the same would be faster, so its source would be stronger and more powerful, so the source of the first would not be infinite in intensity, and the presupposed is contrary. But the occurrence of motion not in time, but in an instant, is impossible; because every motion is over a divisible distance, so it divides by its division, and its measure, I mean time, is also divided.

And an objection was raised against it: We do not accept that traversing that distance in half of that time is possible in reality, and the possibility of assuming its traversal is useless, because it is possible that what is assumed is impossible entailing another impossibility. As for the infinite forever in duration or number, the theologians have allowed it, because the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hellfire have no limit in intensity; due to what you have known that what is meant by infinite in intensity is that an effect stronger than it is not possible, and its description as infinite is considered because it cannot be actualized except after the occurrence of all infinite divisions and their emergence from potentiality to actuality; not that the intensity has not reached the extreme. And know that this proof is more general in scope than the claim; because it proves the impossibility of the existence of a motion that is the fastest of motions, whether it proceeds from a physical or a non-physical force. And the specification in the claim is based on the fact that it is the intended object of the proof.

His statement: (And an objection was raised against it, etc.) Some verifiers answered it: That infinity in intensity entails that the intellect does not deem possible what is stronger than it; so it would not be infinite in intensity, because increase upon the infinite that is systematically ordered in the direction that was infinite negates infinity. And there is in it: That the intellect's deeming possible of the stronger, as a deeming possible conforming to reality, is denied, and the hypothetical deeming possible is of no use.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that a force infinite in intensity is impossible because it would require motion to occur in no time, which is impossible. The passage begins by stating the thesis: physical forces produce motion over a fixed distance in varying times; the shorter the time, the stronger the force. If a force were infinite in intensity, the motion it produces would take zero time. But if motion occurred in time, that time would be divisible, and half that time would produce faster motion, implying a stronger source, contradicting the assumption that the original source is infinite. Therefore, such a force cannot exist. The passage then records an objection: the opponent denies that traversing the distance in half the time is actually possible; hypothetical division does not prove actual possibility. The commentator then provides an answer from some verifiers: infinity in intensity means that no stronger force is even conceivable to the intellect, so if a stronger were conceivable, the original would not be infinite. But the answer is criticized because actual conceivability is not granted; mere hypothetical conceivability is irrelevant. The passage also clarifies that theologians permit infinite duration and number (e.g., eternal bliss and punishment) without intensity implying actual division. The proof is noted to be broader than the claim, as it would negate the fastest motion from any source, not just physical forces.

اللاتناهي في الشدة
الحركة
الزمان
الآن
المنقسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Such a force would require infinite space, which does not exist.
C) The theologians have proven that no force can be infinite.
D) All motion requires time, so infinite intensity is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove that a force infinite in intensity cannot exist because it would require motion in no time, which is impossible given the nature of motion and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that any physical being, by nature, has finite capabilities. Do you live as if your own physical strength, intellect, and lifespan are limited, relying on Allah's infinite power, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The philosophers argued that infinite intensity in a physical force leads to contradictions. In your life, where do you place unrealistic expectations on yourself or others—demanding perfection or infinite capacity from finite beings? How can acknowledging this limitation redirect your trust toward Allah alone?
?
If instantaneous motion is impossible for physical bodies, then every achievement, every step of growth, requires time and effort. Does your daily schedule reflect that you accept this created limitation, or do you seek shortcuts that ignore the process Allah has built into the world?
