A) Because they are impossible
B) Because they are mental considerations
C) Because they are real
D) Because they are external existents

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are mental concepts, not real external entities, so they do not constitute a valid counterexample to the impossibility of circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You treat many concepts in your life as if they are real and independent—like your reputation, your relationships, your plans. But this lesson shows that correlatives are mental considerations, not real dependencies. In what area of your life are you most deceived by a mental construct that feels real, so much so that you forget your complete dependence on Allah? What would it take for you to tear down that mental idol and see it as the mere concept it is?
?
The passage distinguishes between real dependency and mental attribution. You know intellectually that everything depends on Allah, but your actions often betray a sense of self-sufficiency. When you feel proud of your achievements, are you not, like the objector, treating your actions as a correlative to your effort, as if they are real co-causes with Allah? How can you break that pride today, starting with a concrete act of gratitude that acknowledges your absolute need for Him?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

The Impossibility of Circularity and Two Meanings of Dependency
The author argues that circularity is impossible and explains two meanings of dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circularity (al-dawr) is impossible and how the term 'dependency' (al-iftiqār) has two distinct meanings in this context.

Core Idea
Circularity is impossible; dependency can mean inseparability or inseparability with posteriority.

This passage tackles a subtle objection in the proof for the existence of a necessary being. The author clarifies that circularity is logically impossible, but then addresses a doubt by distinguishing two senses of 'dependency'. This distinction is crucial for the argument to hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from the assertion of impossibility to the detailed discussion of dependency.
2
Identify the two meanings of dependency
The passage distinguishes dependency as mere inseparability (imtināʿ al-infikāk) and dependency as inseparability with posteriority (taʾakhkhur). Find where each is introduced.
3
Understand the doubt and its resolution
The doubt is that if cause and effect depend on each other, that would be circular. The author shows that if dependency is mere inseparability, it can be mutual without harm. If it includes posteriority, the doubt recurs.
4
Examine the analysis of the doubt
Notice how the author dissects the meaning of 'posteriority' in the second sense. This shows the precision of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد السادس : الدور ممتنع إما بالضرورة أو بالاستدلال فضلاً عن أن يفتقر كل إلى الآخر، ( أو ) نقول (تلازمهما) على تقدير كونهما موجودين؛ لوحدة السبب الذي يقتضيهما، لا لافتقار كل منهما إلى صاحبه، فلا نقض بهما بوجه قال صاحب اللباب : (ومع ما سبق من جواب شبهة الإمام) على تقدم العلة (فإن بالافتقار) الذي هو مبنى الدليل المرضي عنده امتناع الانفكاك مطلقاً، (فقد يتعاكس الافتقار بهذا المعنى من الجانبين لجواز أن يمتنع انفكاك كل من الشيئين عن الآخر، ولا امتناع في ذلك، بل هو واقع بين المتلازمين، وليس يلزم من تعاكس هذا المعنى بين المعلول والعلة إلا امتناع انفكاك كل منهما عن الآخر، ولا محذور فيه (وإن أريد) بالافتقار امتناع الانفكاك (مع نعت المتأخر أي تأخر المفتقر عن المفتقر إليه)، (جاء في التأخر أعني تأخر المفتقر الذي هو المعلول) (ما جاء من الشبهة في التقدم) أعني تقدم المفتقر إليه الذي هو العلة بعينه؛ إذ يصير حاصل الدليل حينئذ : أن المفتقر أي المعلول متأخر عن العلة، فلو كانت العلة معلولة له لافتقرت أي تأخرت عنه، فيلزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمرتبتين، فيقال : إن أردت بتأخر المعلول معنى المعلولية، كان قولك : لزم تأخر الشيء عن معلوله جارياً مجري قولك : لزم معلولية الشيء لمعلوله، فيمنع بطلانه؛ لأنه عين المتنازع فيه . وإن أردت به معنى آخر فلا بد من تصويره وتقريره فالشبهة مشتركة بين الدليلين المردود والمرضي.
The Fifth Observation - Sixth Objective: Circularity is impossible either necessarily or by demonstration, let alone that each depends on the other. Or we say: They (i.e., cause and effect) are mutually entailing, on the assumption that they exist, due to the unity of the cause that necessitates them, not because each depends on the other; thus there is no refutation of it at all. The author of al-Lubab said: 'Along with what preceded of the answer to the Imam's doubt about the priority of the cause, (for by dependency) which is the basis of the approved proof according to him, inseparability absolutely (so dependency in this sense may be reciprocal from both sides because it is possible that each of two things is inseparable from the other, and there is no impossibility in that; rather it actually occurs between mutually entailing things. From this reciprocal sense between effect and cause nothing follows except the inseparability of each from the other, and there is no harm in it. If what is intended by dependency is inseparability with the attribute of posteriority—i.e., the posteriority of the dependent thing to that on which it depends—then regarding the posteriority (i.e., the posteriority of the dependent which is the effect) the same doubt arises that came regarding the priority (i.e., the priority of that on which it depends which is the cause). For then the substance of the proof becomes: The dependent, i.e., the effect, is posterior to the cause. If the cause were an effect of it, it would depend on it, i.e., be posterior to it, leading to the thing being posterior to itself by two degrees. It is said: If by 'posteriority of the effect' you mean effectness (ma'luliyya), then your statement 'the thing is posterior to its effect' is equivalent to your statement 'the thing is an effect of its effect', which is refuted because it is the very point of dispute. If you mean another meaning, it must be depicted and established. Thus the doubt is common to both the rejected proof and the approved one.'
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage defines the impossibility of circularity (al-dawr) and clarifies two meanings of dependency (al-iftiqār). The author states that circularity is impossible either self-evidently or by demonstration, to say nothing of each depending on the other. He then presents an alternative: we could say cause and effect are mutually entailing (talāzum) due to a single cause that necessitates both, not because each depends on the other. This avoids the problem of circularity. The author of al-Lubab is quoted to explain that dependency can mean simple inseparability (imtināʿ al-infikāk). In this sense, dependency can be mutual between two things—like necessary concomitants (mutalāzimān)—without any impossibility. However, if dependency is taken to include posteriority (taʾakhkhur) of the dependent, then the old doubt returns: if the cause were an effect of its effect, circularity would entail self-posteriority by two degrees. The response is that if 'posteriority' means the same as 'being an effect', the objection is just the point at issue; if it means something else, it must be clarified. Thus the doubt applies equally to both the rejected proof and the approved one.

Key Terms
الدور
الافتقار
التلازم
امتناع الانفكاك
المتلازمين
الشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual dependency always implies circularity.
Correction: If dependency is understood as mere inseparability without posteriority, it can be reciprocal without logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between the two meanings of dependency (iftiqār)?
A) The first meaning implies posteriority, the second does not.
B) The first meaning is inseparability without posteriority, the second adds posteriority.
C) The first meaning applies to causes, the second to effects.
D) The first meaning is used by the Imam, the second by the author.

Key Takeaway
Circularity is impossible, but dependency as mere inseparability can be reciprocal without logical harm, whereas dependency with posteriority leads to a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a habit or attachment you struggle with—something you feel you cannot live without. Is that attachment based on mere inseparability (like a necessary concomitant) or on a belief that it provides you with something prior? How does the distinction between these two types of dependency help you reorient your heart toward Allah alone?
?
You often rely on people, possessions, or routines for your sense of stability. If you were to reframe that dependency as mere inseparability (i.e., they are just always present with you) rather than as a cause of your happiness, how would your gratitude and reliance on Allah change? What step can you take today to break a perceived 'vicious circle' of dependency and turn to Allah as the sole source?

The Efficient Cause Must Exist Simultaneously with Its Effect
The author states a necessary condition for refuting infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the premise that the efficient cause must be simultaneous with its effect, and how this premise is crucial for refuting certain types of infinite regress.

Core Idea
The efficient cause must be present with its effect to avoid separation.

This lesson introduces a foundational premise in the refutation of infinite regress: the efficient cause must exist at the same time as its effect. Without this, cause and effect could be separated, undermining the proof. The author clarifies that this premise applies specifically to independent efficient causes and is only needed for some arguments against infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the premise is stated, then the consequence of denying it is explained, followed by a clarification of which proofs depend on it.
The passage is dense; read it slowly and refer back to the commentary.
2
Understand the premise
The premise is: the efficient cause must be present at the time the effect exists. If it were not necessary, the cause could exist without the effect, and the effect could exist without the cause, leading to separation.
This is a necessary condition for causal connection in the discourse on infinite regress.
3
Identify which proofs depend on it
The commentary lists four proofs used to refute infinite regress. Note that only the first and third (in some cases) depend on this premise; the second (proof of application) and fourth (correlatives) do not.
This shows the premise is not universally required but is essential for the standard proof from causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد السابع : العلة المؤثرة مع المعلول في بيان مقدمة يتوقف عليها إبطال التسلسل، وهي أن تقول : (العلة) المؤثرة أن تكون موجودة (مع المعلول، أي في زمان وجوده، ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يجب ذلك، بل جاز أن يوجد المعلول في زمان ولم توجد العلة في ذلك الزمان، بل قبله ( فقد افترقا أي جاز افتراقهما، فيكون عند وجود العلة لا معلول، وعند وجود المعلول لا علة. قوله : ( يتوقف عليها إبطال التسلسل ) المراد بالتسلسل ما عرفه بقوله وهو أن يستند الممكن إلخ وبالتوقف التوقف في الجملة ولو باعتبار بعض الأدلة أما الأول فظاهر لأن التسلسل الذي لا يكون في العلل المؤثرة لا يتوقف إبطاله على كون العلة المؤثرة مع المعلول وأما الثاني فتفصيله أن الوجه الأول يتوقف على هذه المقدمة والوجه الثاني أعني برهان التطبيق ليس متوقفا عليها لجريانه في الأمور الموجودة متعاقبة كانت أو مجتمعة والوجه الثالث يتوقف عليها لو أجري في تسلسل العلل لأنه الأمور المتعددة الموجودة معا كما سيجيء والوجه الرابع لا يتوقف عليها أصلا لأنه جار في تسلسل المتضايفات ولا يتوقف على كونها موجودة أو معدومة. قوله : (العلة المؤثرة) أي المستقلة بالتأثير وإنما لم يصرح به، لأن ما ليس بمستقلة ليست بمؤثرة في الحقيقة بل بعضها. قوله : (يجب أن تكون موجودة إلخ) أي يجب أن تكون باعتبار وجودها الذي به يؤثر مقارناً للوجود الذي هو أثرها وهذا القدر كاف لنا في إجراء الوجه لأنه يكون آحاد السلسلة حينئذ مجتمعة في الوجود فيكون المجموع موجوداً.
The Fifth Observation - Seventh Objective: The efficient cause (is) with the effect. On explaining a premise upon which depends the refutation of infinite regress, which is to say: The efficient cause must exist with the effect, i.e., at the time of its existence. Otherwise, i.e., if that is not necessary, but it is possible for the effect to exist at a time while the cause does not exist at that time but before it, then they have separated, i.e., their separation is possible; thus at the time of the cause's existence there is no effect, and at the time of the effect's existence there is no cause. His saying: 'Upon which depends the refutation of infinite regress'—what is meant by infinite regress is what he defined by his saying: 'that the contingent depends...' etc. And by 'dependence' is meant dependence in general, even if only with respect to some proofs. As for the former, it is obvious because infinite regress that is not in efficient causes does not have its refutation depend on the efficient cause being with the effect. As for the latter, its detail is: The first proof depends on this premise; the second proof, namely the proof of application (burhān al-taṭbīq), does not depend on it because it applies to existing things whether successive or simultaneous; the third proof depends on it if applied to the regress of causes because they are multiple things existing together, as will come; the fourth proof does not depend on it at all because it applies to the regress of correlatives and does not depend on them being existent or non-existent, only on them being simultaneous. His saying: 'The efficient cause'—i.e., the independent in effecting; it was not explicitly stated because that which is not independent is not truly effective but rather part of it. His saying: 'Must exist...' i.e., it must, in terms of its existence by which it effects, be simultaneous with the existence which is its effect. And this much is sufficient for us to execute the proof because, in that case, the individuals of the series are then simultaneous in existence, so the totality exists.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the premise that the efficient cause (al-ʿilla al-muʾaththira) must be simultaneous with its effect. The author states this as a necessary condition for refuting infinite regress (tasalsul): if the cause could precede its effect, they could be separated, and at the time the cause exists there would be no effect, and vice versa. The commentary clarifies that 'infinite regress' here refers to a regress of efficient causes. The dependence (tawaqquf) of the refutation on this premise is partial: only the first proof (out of four) relies on it; the second proof (burhān al-taṭbīq) does not, the third does if applied to causal regress, and the fourth (about correlatives) does not require simultaneity of existence. The commentary also specifies that 'efficient cause' means the independent cause (al-mustaqilla bi-l-taʾthīr). The premise ensures that all members of the causal series are simultaneous in existence, making the total sum existent, which allows for the refutation of infinite regress.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise requires the cause to exist at every moment of the effect's existence.
Correction: The premise only requires simultaneity at the beginning; that suffices for the refutation of infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The efficient cause must be simultaneous with its effect for the refutation of infinite regress to hold in causal chains.

Simultaneity of Cause and Effect at All Times
Refutation of objection requiring simultaneity only at the beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the efficient cause must be simultaneous with its effect at all times of the effect's existence, not just at its beginning.

Core Idea
The effect depends on the cause at every moment, not just the first.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial objection to the premise that the efficient cause must be simultaneous with its effect. The objection claims that simultaneity is only needed at the beginning, but the author shows this would undermine the refutation of infinite regress. By establishing that the effect needs the cause for its continuance, the author affirms that simultaneity is required at all times.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Get a feel for the structure: objection, response, and final proof.
Notice the use of technical terms like بقاء (continuance) and ابتداء (beginning).
2
Identify the objection
The objector says the premise of refuting infinite regress requires simultaneity only at the beginning. Find this claim and note why it might seem plausible.
The objection is introduced with 'وما قيل'.
3
Understand the delusion (وهم)
The author calls the objection a delusion. Trace the reasoning: if simultaneity is only at the beginning, then later causes could act without the earlier causes being present, breaking the chain needed to refute regress.
The key is that the cause's cause would not be present at later times.
4
Follow the commentator's reasoning
The commentator clarifies that the original text's phrasing suggests gathering at some time, but to fully establish the refutation, we must argue that the effect needs the cause for its continuance (بقاء). This strengthens the premise to require simultaneity at all times.
The commentator builds on the principle that the effect needs the cause in its continuance just as at its origin.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The lesson ends with the establishment that the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with the effect at all times of the effect's existence, completing the proof against infinite regress.
This is a key step in the overall argument about causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قيل: إن مقدمة إبطال التسلسل وجوب في جميع أزمان وجود المعلول لا في ابتداء وجوده فقط وإلا لا يلزم اجتماع العلل بأسرها في الوجود وإبطال التسلسل مبني عليه فوهم منشؤه أنه حينئذ يجوز أن يكون العلة باعتبار وجودها في الزمان الثاني مؤثراً في وجود المعلول وعلة العلة مجتمعة في ابتداء وجودها ولا تكون مجتمعة في الزمان الثاني لأن مقارنة العلة مع المعلول إنما يجب في ابتداء وجود العلة. قوله : (العلة المؤثرة يجب أن تكون موجودة لا شك أن مقدمة إبطال التسلسل وجود في جميع أزمان المعلول لا في ابتداء وجوده فقط وإلا لا يلزم اجتماع العلل بأسرها في الوجود وإبطال التسلسل مبني عليه كما سيأتي لكن ظاهر قوله في الدليل فيكون عند وجود العلة لا معلول وكذا سياق اعتراضه يشعر بأن المراد وجوب اجتماعها مع المعلول ولو في بعض أزمانه فينبغي أن يقال : لما ثبت وجوب مقارنة الوجود للإيجاد، وقد سبق أن المعلول يحتاج إلى العلة في بقائه، كما هو محتاج إليها في ابتداء وجوده ثبت وجوب مقارنة وجودها لوجود المعلول في جميع أزمانه ويتم المطلوب.
And what was said: That the premise for refuting infinite regress is obligatory in all times of the effect's existence, not only at the beginning of its existence, otherwise the gathering of all causes in existence would not be necessary, and the refutation of infinite regress is based on it. That is a delusion, whose source is that then it would be permissible that the cause, by virtue of its existence in the second time, be effective in the existence of the effect, while the cause of the cause is gathered at the beginning of its existence and is not gathered in the second time, because the simultaneity of the cause with the effect is only obligatory at the beginning of the cause's existence. His saying: 'The efficient cause must exist' — there is no doubt that the premise for refuting infinite regress is existence in all times of the effect, not only at the beginning of its existence; otherwise the gathering of all causes in existence would not be necessary, and the refutation of infinite regress is based on it, as will come. But the apparent meaning of his statement in the proof: 'then at the time of the cause's existence there is no effect' and likewise the context of his objection indicate that what is intended is the necessity of its being gathered with the effect even if only in some of its times. So it should be said: Since the necessity of the simultaneity of existence for bringing-into-existence (ījād) has been established, and it has preceded that the effect needs the cause in its continuance (baqāʾ) just as it needs it at the beginning of its existence, the necessity of the simultaneity of its existence with the existence of the effect in all its times is established, and the desired end is achieved.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection to the premise introduced earlier. Someone objected that the premise (that the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect) must hold for all times of the effect's existence, not just at its beginning; otherwise the entire series of causes would not be gathered in existence simultaneously, and the refutation of infinite regress depends on that simultaneous gathering. The author calls this a delusion (wahm). Its source is the mistaken idea that if simultaneity is only required at the beginning, then the cause could be effective in a later time while the cause's cause is only present at the beginning, breaking the simultaneous chain. The commentator then clarifies: the original text's phrasing ('then at the time of the cause's existence there is no effect') suggests that the intended meaning is that the cause must be gathered with the effect at some time, not necessarily all. However, to fully achieve the refutation, one should argue that since the effect needs the cause both for its origination and its continuance (baqāʾ), the cause must be simultaneous with the effect at all times of its existence. This strengthens the premise and completes the desired conclusion.

The Efficient Cause Must Coexist with Its Effect
Al-Jurjani states the premise that the efficient cause must coexist with its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal coexistence of cause and effect is necessary for causality, and how this premise refutes infinite regress.

Core Idea
A cause that does not coexist with its effect is no cause at all.

This passage from al-Jurjani establishes a foundational premise for refuting infinite regress in causal chains. Before we can argue about the impossibility of an endless series of causes, we must first agree on what it means for something to be a cause. Al-Jurjani clarifies that a true efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect. This logical requirement will later underpin arguments for the existence of a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Notice that the passage states the premise explicitly and then shows the consequence if it is violated.
The key term is 'العلة المؤثرة' (efficient cause).
2
Understand the logical argument
The author argues that if cause and effect are not simultaneous, they are separated. That separation means at the time of the cause there is no effect, and at the time of the effect there is no cause. Therefore, the effect cannot be attributed to that cause.
This directly refutes the idea that a past cause can produce a later effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد السابع : العلة المؤثرة مع المعلول في بيان مقدمة يتوقف عليها إبطال التسلسل، وهي أن تقول : العلة المؤثرة أن تكون موجودة مع المعلول، أي في زمان وجوده، وإلا أي وإن لم يجب ذلك، بل جاز أن يوجد المعلول في زمان ولم توجد العلة في ذلك الزمان، بل قبله فقد افترقا أي جاز افتراقهما، فيكون عند وجود العلة لا معلول، وعند وجود المعلول لا علة فليس وجوده لوجودها فلا علية بينهما.
The fifth observation - the seventh section: the efficient cause with the effect. On explaining a premise on which the refutation of infinite regress depends, which is that you say: the efficient cause must exist with the effect, i.e., in the time of its existence. Otherwise—i.e., if that is not necessary, but it is permitted that the effect exists in a time and the cause does not exist in that time but before it—then they have separated, i.e., their separation is permitted. So at the existence of the cause there is no effect, and at the existence of the effect there is no cause. Thus its existence is not because of its existence, so there is no causality between them.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage defines the premise needed to refute infinite regress. The author first states the section title, then explains that the premise is: the efficient cause must exist simultaneously with its effect. He clarifies that this means the cause must be present in the same time as the effect's existence. If this is not required—if the effect could exist in a time while the cause existed only earlier—then cause and effect are separated. In that case, when the cause exists, the effect does not yet exist, and when the effect exists, the cause is no longer present. Therefore, the effect's existence is not due to the cause's existence, and no causal relationship holds. This establishes the necessity of temporal coexistence for causality.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة
المعلول
التسلسل
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause must only precede its effect in time to be its cause.
Correction: This passage argues that temporal precedence alone is insufficient. The cause must exist simultaneously with the effect; otherwise, the effect's existence is not dependent on that cause.

Checkpoint

B) Cause and effect would be separated, so no causality exists
C) The cause must be eternal
D) The effect would cause itself

Key Takeaway
For causality to hold, the efficient cause must coexist temporally with its effect; otherwise, no causal relationship exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you commit to pausing before attributing effects to distant causes, and instead remember the One who must be present with every effect, sustaining it at every instant?

Objection and Answer on Temporal Separation
Sanusi presents an objection and a two-part answer regarding temporal separation of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument against temporal separation between cause and effect and why it leads to either identity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
Cause and effect cannot be temporally separated without contradiction.

This passage presents a key objection to the principle that cause and effect must be simultaneous. The objector suggests that the cause can produce the effect later. Sanusi responds with a powerful analysis that shows this leads to an impossible regress. Let's examine the structure of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage first, then the translation. Note how the objector separates the influence (first time) from the effect (second time).
2
Analyze the first branch of the answer
Sanusi considers two possibilities. First, if the causing is identical to the effect's occurrence, then they must be simultaneous.
This is straightforward.
3
Analyze the second branch
If causing is distinct from the effect, then it requires another causing, leading to infinite regress. Understand why regress is impossible.
This refutes the objection.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection fails because either way, temporal separation is impossible. This strengthens the principle of simultaneous causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا يلزم من افتراقهما أن لا يكون وجود المعلول لأجل وجود العلة؛ إذ لعلها أي العلة في الزمان الأول الذي هو زمان وجودها توجد المعلول أي تحصل وجوده في الزمان الثاني، فيكون التأثير والإيجاد في الزمان الأول، والتأثر وحصول المعلول في الزمان الثاني. قلنا : الإيجاد أي إيجاد العلة للمعلول وإيجابها إياه إن كان نفس حصول المعلول، فلا يتخلف حصول المعلول عنه ، أي عن إيجاب العلة إياه؛ لامتناع تخلف الشي عن نفسه. وإن كان الإيجاد والإيجاب غيره أي غير حصول المعلول كان ذلك الغير الذي هو الإيجاب موجبا في الحال له أي لحصول ذلك المعلول، في ثاني الحال فله أي فلذلك الغير وهو الإيجاب إيجاب آخر. وينقل الكلام إلى إيجاب الإيجاب وتتسلسل الإيجابات إلى غير النهاية.
If it is said: Their separation does not necessitate that the existence of the effect is not due to the existence of the cause; for perhaps the cause in the first time, which is the time of its existence, brings about the effect, i.e., obtains its existence, in the second time. So the influence and causing occur in the first time, and the being influenced and the occurrence of the effect in the second time. We say: The causing, i.e., the cause's bringing about the effect and its necessitating it, if it is identical to the occurrence of the effect, then the occurrence of the effect is not separate from it, i.e., from the cause's necessitating it, because it is impossible for a thing to be separate from itself. And if the causing and necessitating is other than it, i.e., other than the occurrence of the effect, then that other, which is the necessitating, is necessitating in the present moment for it, i.e., for the occurrence of that effect, in the second moment. So that other, i.e., the necessitating, has another necessitating. And the discussion is transferred to the necessitating of the necessitating, and the necessitations regress infinitely.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that even if cause and effect are separated in time, causality is still possible: the cause exists in the first time and produces the effect in the second time. The author responds by analyzing the nature of causing (إيجاد). He considers two possibilities. First, if the causing is identical to the effect's occurrence, then the effect's occurrence cannot be separate from the causing, so it must occur at the same time as the cause, contradicting the objection. Second, if the causing is something other than the effect's occurrence, then that causing (which is a separate entity) itself requires another causing to bring it about, leading to an infinite regress of causings, which is impossible. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
الإيجاد
الإيجاب
المعلول
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that cause can act at a distance in time, producing its effect later.
Correction: The argument shows that this leads to either identity of causing and effect (no separation) or an infinite regress of causings, both impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative considered in the answer?
A) The causing is separate from the effect
B) The causing is identical to the effect's occurrence
C) The causing is in the second time
D) The causing is an accident

Key Takeaway
Temporal separation between cause and effect is impossible; it leads to either identity or an infinite regress of causings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan for the future, do you assume that your current actions will automatically produce desired results later? How does this lesson's demonstration that cause and effect cannot be temporally separated challenge your assumption that you can control outcomes across time?
?
If the causing and the effect must be simultaneous, what does that imply about your reliance on secondary causes like your job, health, or relationships? Are you subconsciously treating them as independent causes with delayed effects?
?
How does the idea that every causing requires a simultaneous cause (or else infinite regress) affect your understanding of Allah's role as the ultimate Cause? Are you living as if Allah is directly causing every moment, or do you functionally believe in a chain of temporally separated causes?

Regress of Causings in Mental Considerations
Al-Jurjani refutes an infinite regress objection by appealing to mental considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of causings is considered acceptable within the realm of mental considerations.

Core Idea
Not all infinite regresses are vicious—only real ones. Mental regress is harmless.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary tackles a subtle objection: if every causing requires another causing, we get an infinite regress. Al-Jurjani clarifies that this regress is not a problem because it exists only in the mind, not in external reality. Understanding this distinction is key to many arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the structure: a consideration, then a reason, then two ways of rejecting the regress, then the final resolution.
2
Identify the objection being answered
The passage responds to the claim that causings would regress infinitely. Locate the statement that the necessitating is not itself necessitating.
This is the key: the causing does not itself need a cause.
3
Understand the two arguments against the regress
The author says the regress is invalid either by intuition (we know no effect comes from an infinite chain) or by a proof that avoids circularity.
These are two independent ways to reject the regress.
4
Grasp the final distinction
Even if the regress occurred, it would be in mental considerations, which is acceptable. This diffuses the objection.
The key term is 'umūr iʿtibāriyya' — conceptual entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر؛ لأنه أي الإيجاب على تقدير المغايرة ليس موجبا حتى يلزم أن يكون له إيجاب آخر. وتتسلسل الإيجابات إلى غير النهاية. وهذا التسلسل باطل إما بالبديهة لأنا نعلم قطعاً أنه لا يصدر حين صدور أثر أمور غير متناهية، وإما ببرهان لا يتوقف على هذه المقدمة لئلا يلزم المصادرة. ويندفع الاعتراض بأنه تسلسل في الأمور الاعتبارية مع أنه في جانب المعلول وهو ملتزم.
And in it there is consideration; because the necessitating, on the assumption of difference, is not necessitating such that it would necessitate another necessitating. And the necessitations regress infinitely. And this regress is invalid either by intuition, for we know with certainty that when an effect occurs, it does not arise from an infinite number of things, or by a proof that does not depend on this premise, to avoid circularity. And the objection is repelled by it being a regress in mental considerations, and it is on the side of the effect, and it is accepted.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents a consideration (نظر) on the previous argument that the causing leads to infinite regress. The author points out that the necessitating (الإيجاب) on the supposition of being different from the effect is not itself a necessitating that requires another necessitating. Then he notes that the infinite regress of causings is invalid—either self-evidently, because we know that no effect arises from an infinite chain, or by a proof that does not rely on the premise in question (to avoid circularity). This refutes the objection that the regress is infinite, and it is further clarified that the regress is in mental considerations (أمور اعتبارية), i.e., conceptual entities, and as it occurs on the side of the effect, it is admissible (ملتزم). Thus, the objection does not undermine the original argument.

التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All infinite regresses are impossible or vicious.
Correction: This passage shows that regress in mental considerations (conceptual entities) is acceptable and not a problem for the argument.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for rejecting the infinite regress of causings?
A) It is impossible by intuition
B) It leads to contradiction
C) It is not a real regress but mental
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Regress in mental considerations (umūr iʿtibāriyya) does not entail a vicious infinite regress in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Commit to resting in the sufficiency of Allah's attributes as they are, without demanding causal explanations that lead nowhere.
?

Examine your own heart: do you truly feel the intuitive impossibility of an infinite regress in reality, or do you just accept it as a theological position? Let this passage push you to strengthen your inner conviction until it becomes a living certainty.
?
This lesson distinguishes between mental constructs and external reality. In your life, do you confuse mental worries (like ‘how does God’s will relate to my actions?’) with actual external problems? Identify one such confusion you currently hold—perhaps regarding destiny or causality—and decide to treat it as a mere mental consideration, not a real barrier to faith.

Can Cause Precede Effect?
Al-Iji presents an objection and initial answer about cause-effect timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why cause and effect cannot be separated in time without leading to an infinite regress of necessitations.

Core Idea
Separating cause and effect in time leads to an infinite regress of causes.

Al-Iji presents a critical objection to the necessity of simultaneity between cause and effect. The objection suggests that a cause could exist first and produce its effect later. In this passage, Al-Iji offers the initial answer, showing that this separation would force an infinite regress of acts of causation, thereby preserving the principle that cause and effect are simultaneous.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the claim that cause could exist at time one and produce the effect at time two.
Note the phrasing: 'perhaps the cause ... brings into existence the effect in the second time.'
2
Identify the two cases
The answer splits into two possibilities: the act of causing (إيجاد) is either identical to the effect's occurrence or distinct from it.
3
Trace the first case
If causing is identical to the effect's occurrence, then the effect cannot lag behind the causing, as a thing cannot lag behind itself.
This case shows simultaneity is necessary.
4
Trace the second case
If causing is distinct from the effect, then that distinct act of causing itself requires another act of causing, leading to an infinite regress.
This regress is absurd, so the only coherent option is simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد السابع : العلة المؤثرة مع المعلول
المعلول لا علة فليس وجوده لوجودها فلا علية بينهما . ( فإن قيل : لا يلزم من افتراقهما أن لا يكون وجود المعلول لأجل وجود العلة؛ إذ لعلها أي العلة في الزمان الأول الذي هو زمان وجودها توجد المعلول أي تحصل وجوده في الزمان الثاني، فيكون التأثير والإيجاد في الزمان الأول، والتأثر وحصول المعلول في الزمان الثاني . ( قلنا : الإيجاد أي إيجاد العلة للمعلول وإيجابها إياه إن كان نفس حصول المعلول، فلا يتخلف حصول المعلول عنه ، أي عن إيجاب العلة إياه ؛ لامتناع تخلف الشي عن نفسه . وإن كان الإيجاد والإيجاب غيره أي غير حصول المعلول كان ذلك الغير الذي هو الإيجاب موجبا في الحال له أي لحصول ذلك المعلول، في ثاني الحال فله أي فلذلك الغير وهو الإيجاب إيجاب آخر. وينقل الكلام إلى إيجاب الإيجاب وتتسلسل الإيجابات إلى غير النهاية.
The Fifth Observation - Seventh Aim: The Efficient Cause with the Effect. The effect is not a cause, so its existence is not for the existence of the cause, so no causality between them. If it is said: It does not follow from their separation that the existence of the effect is not because of the existence of the cause; for perhaps the cause in the first time, which is the time of its existence, brings into existence the effect, i.e., produces its existence in the second time, so the affecting and bringing into existence is in the first time, and the being affected and the occurrence of the effect is in the second time. We say: The causing-to-exist, i.e., the cause's bringing the effect into existence and its necessitating it, if it is the very occurrence of the effect, then the occurrence of the effect does not lag behind it, i.e., behind the cause's necessitating it; because it is impossible for a thing to lag behind itself. And if the causing-to-exist and necessitating is other than it, i.e., other than the occurrence of the effect, then that other, which is the necessitation, is itself a cause in the present moment for it, i.e., for the occurrence of that effect in the second moment, so that other, i.e., the necessitation, has another necessitation. And the discussion is transferred to the necessitation of the necessitation, and the necessitations go on in infinite regress.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the claim that the efficient cause must be simultaneous with its effect. The objector suggests that the cause could exist at time one and produce the effect at time two. The author (al-Iji) gives the initial answer: if the act of causing is identical to the effect's occurrence, then the effect cannot be separated from it. If the act of causing is distinct, then that distinct act itself requires another cause to produce it, leading to an infinite regress of causes. The passage thus establishes that proximity between cause and effect is necessary; otherwise, an infinite chain of causes would result.

الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect can be separated in time without any problem.
Correction: The passage shows that separation leads to an infinite regress of acts of causation, thus they must be simultaneous.

Checkpoint
According to the initial answer, what follows if the act of causing-to-exist (الإيجاد) is distinct from the occurrence of the effect?
A) The effect occurs without a cause.
B) The cause and effect are simultaneous.
C) An infinite regress of necessitations arises.
D) The effect does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Cause and effect must be simultaneous; otherwise an infinite regress of acts of causation arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Critique of the Regress Argument
Al-Iji critiques the regress argument by denying that the necessitation is a cause and invoking a priori necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's critique that the necessitation (الإيجاب) is not a cause and that a priori necessity distinguishes cause from effect.

Core Idea
The act of causing is not itself a cause; otherwise regress is unavoidable.

Al-Iji begins by examining the regress argument against causality. He points out that if the necessitation (إيجاب) is distinct from the effect, it is not itself a cause that would require another necessitation. Moreover, our intuitive knowledge tells us that the act of causing is not identical to the effect occurring. This lesson unpacks these key points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Identify the two main points: the denial that necessitation is a cause, and the argument from necessity.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were a cause, then regress, but it is not.'
2
Analyze the first point
Understand why the necessitation on the assumption of difference is not a cause.
3
Examine the second point
See how a priori necessity (الضرورة) negates identity between cause and effect.
Everyone knows the truth of the statement 'the cause necessitated it, so it occurred.'
4
Reflect on the false dilemma
The passage says making the necessitation either itself or other is a choice between two things, one impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر؛ لأنه أي الإيجاب على تقدير المغايرة ليس موجباً حتى يلزم أن يكون له إيجاب آخر، بل يكون إيجاباً مغايراً لحصول المعلول، وإلا أي وإن لم يكن كذلك، بل كان الإيجاب موجباً لزم التسلسل في الإيجاب مطلقاً، وسواء كان الإيجاب حال وجود المعلول أو قبله، وسواء كان مغايراً لحصول المعلول أو لم يكن. ولأن الضرورة تنفي كون الإيجاب نفس حصول المعلول إذ كل أحد يعلم صدق قولنا: أوجبه العلة فحصل. فترديد الإيجاب بين أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ترديد بين أمرين أحدهما لازم الانتفاء، وهو مستدرك مستقبح جداً. وقد يجاب بأنه إذا كانت العلة توجب في الحال وجود المعلول في ثاني الحال، فحينئذ لا معلول حال إيجاب العلة، وبالعكس أي لا إيجاب حال حصول المعلول فليس حصوله لإيجابها له. ولما أمكن أن يتطرق إليه المنع المذكور أولاً، قال المصنف: والأولى في دفع
قوله: ( لأنه ليس موجباً قيل: إن الإيجاب أمر متجدد، فلا بد من علة الاتصاف ويتحقق إيجاب آخر ويلزم التسلسل البتة فتدبر. قوله: ( بل كان الإيجاب) أي على تقدير المغايرة موجباً لزم التسلسل مطلقاً لأنه إذا كان الإيجاب مع كونه مغايراً ومتقدماً على وجود المعلول موجباً لأجل استتباعه له فكونه موجباً حال عدم المغايرة والمعية بطريق الأولى لأن الاستتباع حينئذ أقوى. فاندفع ما قيل: إن كون الإيجاب موجباً على تقدير المغايرة والقبلية كيف يستلزم كونه موجباً على تقدير انتفائهما فالصواب ترك قوله وإلا لزم التسلسل مطلقاً.
قوله: ( لازم الانتفاء) أي عند العقل بحيث لا يجوزه. أقول: يمكن توجيه الجواب بحيث لا يرد النظر المذكور بأن يقال: الإيجاد وإن كان مغايراً لحصول الأثر بحسب المفهوم وبهذه المغايرة يصح الترتيب بينهما بالفاء كما في قولك رماه فقتله فهو إما نفس حصول الأثر في الخارج فلا يتخلف عنه أو غيره في الخارج متقدم عليه فهو أمر يوجب حصول المعلول في الزمان الثاني فيكون موجباً وننقل الكلام إلى الإيجاب الثاني وإذا كان غير حصول المعلول في الخارج ومتقدماً عليه كان موجباً لحصوله في الزمان الثاني بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن غيره في الخارج أو لم يكن متقدماً فإنه إيجاب وليس بموجب.
قوله: (لأنه ليس موجباً إلخ) قيل عليه الإيجاب أمر متحقق في محله فلا بد له الاتصاف ويتحقق إيجاب آخر ويلزم التسلسل البتة.
And there is a consideration; because the necessitation, on the assumption of difference, is not a cause such that it must have another necessitation; rather it is a necessitation different from the occurrence of the effect. And if not, i.e., if it were not so, but the necessitation were a cause, then the regress in necessitation would necessarily follow absolutely, whether the necessitation is at the moment of the effect's existence or before it, and whether it is different from the occurrence of the effect or not. And because necessity (a priori knowledge) negates that the necessitation is the very occurrence of the effect, since everyone knows the truth of our statement: 'The cause necessitated it, so it occurred.' So making the necessitation either itself or other than it is a choice between two things, one of which necessarily does not exist, and it is an error and very ugly. And it may be answered that if the cause necessitated in the present moment the existence of the effect in the second moment, then there is no effect at the moment of the cause's necessitation, and vice versa, i.e., no necessitation at the moment of the effect's occurrence, so its occurrence is not due to its necessitation. And when the aforementioned objection could be directed at it, the author said: 'The better way to refute...' His statement: 'Because it is not a cause' – it is said: The necessitation is a renewed thing, so there must be a cause of attribution, and another necessitation would occur, and regress would necessarily follow, so reflect. His statement: 'Rather the necessitation' – i.e., on the assumption of difference, it is a cause; regress would necessarily follow absolutely, because if the necessitation, despite being different and prior to the effect's existence, is a cause due to its entailment of it, then its being a cause in the case of non-difference and simultaneity is with greater reason, because the entailment then is stronger. So what was said is repelled: How does the necessitation being a cause on the assumption of difference and priority necessarily entail its being a cause on the assumption of their absence? So the correct is to leave his statement 'otherwise regress would necessarily follow absolutely.' His statement: 'necessarily does not exist' – i.e., according to reason, such that it does not allow it. I say: It is possible to direct the answer so that the aforementioned consideration does not apply by saying: The causing-to-exist, even if it is different from the occurrence of the effect conceptually, and with this difference the order between them is valid with the letter 'fa' as in your saying 'he shot him so he killed him.' So it is either the very occurrence of the effect in external reality, so it does not lag behind it, or it is other than it in external reality and prior to it, so it is something that necessitates the occurrence of the effect in the second moment, so it is a cause; and we transfer the discussion to the second necessitation. And if it is other than the occurrence of the effect in external reality and prior to it, then it is a cause for its occurrence in the second moment, unlike if it is not other than it in external reality or not prior, then it is a necessitation and not a cause. His statement: 'Because it is not a cause, etc.' – it is said against it: The necessitation is a thing that exists in its subject, so it must have attribution, and another necessitation would occur, and regress would necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage provides a critique of the earlier regress argument and refutes it. The author (al-Iji) first points out that the necessitation (الإيجاب) on the assumption of being distinct from the effect is not itself a cause, so no regress follows. He then argues that intuitive necessity (الضرورة) shows that the act of causing is not identical to the effect, as everyone knows the difference between 'the cause necessitated' and 'the effect occurred.' The author further explains that the better refutation is to say that if the cause necessitates the effect's existence at a later moment, then at the moment of necessitation there is no effect, and at the moment of effect there is no necessitation—so the effect does not arise from the necessitation. The commentary then addresses objections: if the necessitation is a renewed attribute, it would require a cause, leading to regress; but the refutation shows that the necessitation can still be a cause without regress by distinguishing between conceptual difference and external reality. The passage concludes by affirming that the correct view avoids the infinite regress by showing that the causing act, if distinct and prior, is a cause, otherwise it is not.

Key Terms
النظر
التسلسل
الضرورة
الإيجاب
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any distinction between cause and effect leads to an infinite regress.
Correction: The passage shows that if the act of causing is distinct from the effect, it is not itself a cause requiring another act, so regress is avoided.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to deny that the necessitation (الإيجاب) is the same as the occurrence of the effect?
A) Logical demonstration
B) A priori necessity (الضرورة)
C) Scriptural evidence
D) Consensus

Key Takeaway
The act of causing is not a cause; otherwise regress would follow, and intuitive knowledge confirms it is not identical to the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say, 'Allah caused this to happen,' do you truly distinguish between the act of causing and the effect itself, or do you conflate them? How might this confusion affect your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming that the means (like effort or medicine) are the actual causes, forgetting that the real cause is Allah? What would it take for you to see every event as directly necessitated by Him?
?
If the act of causing is not a cause itself, then your own actions are not the ultimate causes of outcomes. Does this liberate you from anxiety or make you feel helpless? How can you embrace both responsibility and reliance on the One True Cause?

Refutation and Defense of the Regress Objection
Al-Iji refutes the regress by temporally separating causation and effect, with commentary addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the regress objection by temporally separating the act of causing from the effect and addressing subsequent objections.

Core Idea
The act of causing occurs in a different moment than the effect, so no regress arises.

After the initial critique, al-Iji presents a stronger refutation: if the cause necessitates the effect in the second moment, then the effect does not exist when the cause acts, and the act of causing does not exist when the effect occurs. This temporal separation diffuses the regress. However, commentators raise objections, questioning whether the act of causing itself requires another cause. This lesson examines the solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the core refutation
Focus on the temporal separation: cause acts in one moment, effect in another.
2
Identify the objection
The objection says the act of causing is a renewed event needing a cause, leading to regress.
This is a common counterargument.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentary clarifies: if the causing act is really distinct and prior, it is a cause; otherwise it is not. The chain stops at the eternal cause.
The distinction between conceptual and real difference is key.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله: ( لازم الانتفاء) أي عند العقل بحيث لا يجوزه. أقول: يمكن توجيه الجواب بحيث لا يرد النظر المذكور بأن يقال: الإيجاد وإن كان مغايراً لحصول الأثر بحسب المفهوم وبهذه المغايرة يصح الترتيب بينهما بالفاء كما في قولك رماه فقتله فهو إما نفس حصول الأثر في الخارج فلا يتخلف عنه أو غيره في الخارج متقدم عليه فهو أمر يوجب حصول المعلول في الزمان الثاني فيكون موجباً وننقل الكلام إلى الإيجاب الثاني وإذا كان غير حصول المعلول في الخارج ومتقدماً عليه كان موجباً لحصوله في الزمان الثاني بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن غيره في الخارج أو لم يكن متقدماً فإنه إيجاب وليس بموجب.
قوله: (لأنه ليس موجباً إلخ) قيل عليه الإيجاب أمر متحقق في محله فلا بد له الاتصاف ويتحقق إيجاب آخر ويلزم التسلسل البتة.
And there is a consideration; because the necessitation, on the assumption of difference, is not a cause such that it must have another necessitation; rather it is a necessitation different from the occurrence of the effect. And if not, i.e., if it were not so, but the necessitation were a cause, then the regress in necessitation would necessarily follow absolutely, whether the necessitation is at the moment of the effect's existence or before it, and whether it is different from the occurrence of the effect or not. And because necessity (a priori knowledge) negates that the necessitation is the very occurrence of the effect, since everyone knows the truth of our statement: 'The cause necessitated it, so it occurred.' So making the necessitation either itself or other than it is a choice between two things, one of which necessarily does not exist, and it is an error and very ugly. And it may be answered that if the cause necessitated in the present moment the existence of the effect in the second moment, then there is no effect at the moment of the cause's necessitation, and vice versa, i.e., no necessitation at the moment of the effect's occurrence, so its occurrence is not due to its necessitation. And when the aforementioned objection could be directed at it, the author said: 'The better way to refute...' His statement: 'Because it is not a cause' – it is said: The necessitation is a renewed thing, so there must be a cause of attribution, and another necessitation would occur, and regress would necessarily follow, so reflect. His statement: 'Rather the necessitation' – i.e., on the assumption of difference, it is a cause; regress would necessarily follow absolutely, because if the necessitation, despite being different and prior to the effect's existence, is a cause due to its entailment of it, then its being a cause in the case of non-difference and simultaneity is with greater reason, because the entailment then is stronger. So what was said is repelled: How does the necessitation being a cause on the assumption of difference and priority necessarily entail its being a cause on the assumption of their absence? So the correct is to leave his statement 'otherwise regress would necessarily follow absolutely.' His statement: 'necessarily does not exist' – i.e., according to reason, such that it does not allow it. I say: It is possible to direct the answer so that the aforementioned consideration does not apply by saying: The causing-to-exist, even if it is different from the occurrence of the effect conceptually, and with this difference the order between them is valid with the letter 'fa' as in your saying 'he shot him so he killed him.' So it is either the very occurrence of the effect in external reality, so it does not lag behind it, or it is other than it in external reality and prior to it, so it is something that necessitates the occurrence of the effect in the second moment, so it is a cause; and we transfer the discussion to the second necessitation. And if it is other than the occurrence of the effect in external reality and prior to it, then it is a cause for its occurrence in the second moment, unlike if it is not other than it in external reality or not prior, then it is a necessitation and not a cause. His statement: 'Because it is not a cause, etc.' – it is said against it: The necessitation is a thing that exists in its subject, so it must have attribution, and another necessitation would occur, and regress would necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage provides a critique of the earlier regress argument and refutes it. The author (al-Iji) first points out that the necessitation (الإيجاب) on the assumption of being distinct from the effect is not itself a cause, so no regress follows. He then argues that intuitive necessity (الضرورة) shows that the act of causing is not identical to the effect, as everyone knows the difference between 'the cause necessitated' and 'the effect occurred.' The author further explains that the better refutation is to say that if the cause necessitates the effect's existence at a later moment, then at the moment of necessitation there is no effect, and at the moment of effect there is no necessitation—so the effect does not arise from the necessitation. The commentary then addresses objections: if the necessitation is a renewed attribute, it would require a cause, leading to regress; but the refutation shows that the necessitation can still be a cause without regress by distinguishing between conceptual difference and external reality. The passage concludes by affirming that the correct view avoids the infinite regress by showing that the causing act, if distinct and prior, is a cause, otherwise it is not.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of causing itself must be caused, leading to infinite regress.
Correction: The act of causing is only a cause if it is really distinct and prior; otherwise it is not, and the chain stops at the eternal cause.

The Better Refutation of the Regress Objection
Al-Iji presents the better refutation and clarifies the relation of cause and effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Iji's better refutation of the regress objection regarding causality, and how he clarifies the relationship between necessitation and the effect.

Core Idea
The causing act is distinct from the effect; this distinction avoids infinite regress.

Al-Iji presents his preferred refutation to the problem of causal regress. He argues that the act of necessitation (الإيجاب) is not identical to the occurrence of the effect, and that on the assumption of identity, the notion of 'necessitation' must be understood in relation to the subsequent necessity (الوجوب اللاحق). This lesson unpacks this nuanced argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the regress objection
Review the initial problem: if necessitation were identical to the effect, an infinite regress would follow. The passage begins by stating this dilemma.
2
Follow the better refutation
Al-Iji responds by showing that if the cause necessitates the effect for a future moment, then at the moment of necessitation there is no effect, breaking the regress.
This is the core of the better refutation.
3
Examine the subtle clarification
On the assumption that the causing act is identical to the effect, the term 'necessitation' is still meaningful when understood in relation to the subsequent necessity (الوجوب اللاحق).
This addresses a potential objection to the identity view.
4
Interpret the key phrase
The commentary clarifies that 'one of them necessarily does not exist' means one of the two options (identity or difference) must be false, but not determinately which at first glance.
This resolves an ambiguity in the original argument.

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on page 164, offering the better refutation (الأولى) of the regress objection. The author reiterates that the necessitation cannot be identical to the effect, as common sense distinguishes them. The better answer is that if the cause necessitates the effect for a future moment, then at the moment of necessitation there is no effect, and vice versa—so the effect does not directly depend on the necessitation in a way that requires regress. The Infinite Regress from a Distinct Causal Act
Presents the regress objection and the dismissal of identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing a distinct act of necessitation (إيجاب) separate from the effect leads to an infinite regress, and why identifying them is impossible by intuitive necessity.

Core Idea
A distinct causal act demands its own cause, leading to endless regression.

This passage begins a dialectical exploration of causality. The author first shows that if the cause's act of necessitation is considered something other than the effect's existence, then an infinite regress of necessitations follows. He then argues that identifying them is impossible by intuitive necessity, leaving a false dichotomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: first a conditional regress argument, then an appeal to intuition.
2
Identify the regress argument
Find the conditional statement: if the necessitation is distinct from the effect, then regress follows. Understand why each distinct necessitation requires its own cause.
3
Examine the intuition argument
See why the author says it is impossible for the necessitation to be identical to the effect. Note the example: 'The cause necessitated it, and it occurred.'
This shows the division is between two impossible or problematic options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيجاب آخر، (بل) يكون ( إيجاباً) مغايراً لحصول المعلول، ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن كذلك، بل كان الإيجاب موجباً ( لزم التسلسل ) في الإيجاب ( مطلقاً)، وسواء كان الإيجاب حال وجود المعلول أو قبله، وسواء كان مغايراً لحصول المعلول أو لم يكن ) ولأن الضرورة تنفي كون الإيجاب (نفس) حصول ( المعلول) إذ كل أحد يعلم صدق قولنا : أوجبه العلة فحصل فترديد الإيجاب بين أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ترديد بين أمرين أحدهما لازم الانتفاء، وهو مستدرك مستقبح جداً. ( وقد يجاب بأنه ) إذا كانت العلة توجب في الحال وجود المعلول في ثاني الحال، فحينئذ ( لا معلول حال إيجاب العلة، وبالعكس ) أي لا إيجاب حال حصول المعلول فليس حصوله لإيجابها له، ولما أمكن أن يتطرق إليه المنع المذكور أولاً ، قال المصنف : ( والأولى ) في دفع
قوله : ( لأنه ليس موجباً قيل : إن الإيجاب أمر متجدد، فلا بد من علة الاتصاف ويتحقق إيجاب آخر ويلزم التسلسل البتة فتدبر .
Another necessitation (إيجاب), but it would be a necessitation different from the occurrence of the effect, and otherwise, i.e., if it were not so, but rather the necessitation were a necessitator, then an infinite regress (تسلسل) would necessarily follow in the necessitation absolutely, whether the necessitation is at the time of the effect's existence or before it, and whether it is different from the occurrence of the effect or not. And because intuitive necessity (الضرورة) negates that the necessitation is the very occurrence of the effect, since everyone knows the truth of our statement: 'The cause necessitated it, and it occurred.' So the division of necessitation between being itself or other is a division between two things, one of which is necessarily non-existent, and it is a preposterous and very ugly fallacy. And it may be answered that when the cause necessitates in the present the existence of the effect in the second moment, then there is no effect at the time of the cause's necessitation, and conversely, i.e., no necessitation at the time of the effect's occurrence, so its occurrence is not due to its necessitation of it. And since the aforementioned objection could potentially be directed against it, the author said: 'And the better (الأولى) way to repel...' His saying: 'Because it is not a necessitator.' It was said: 'Necessitation is a temporally originated matter, so there must be a cause of the attribution, and another necessitation occurs, and regress necessarily follows; so reflect.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument in the problem of the relationship between a cause's act of necessitation (إيجاب) and the effect's existence. The author begins by stating that if necessitation is considered distinct (مغاير) from the occurrence of the effect, then an infinite regress (تسلسل) would necessarily follow, regardless of whether the necessitation is simultaneous with or prior to the effect, and whether it is distinct or not. This is because each distinct necessitation would itself require a cause to bring it about, leading to an endless chain. The author then notes that intuitive necessity (الضرورة) rejects the idea that necessitation is identical to the effect, since we naturally say, 'The cause necessitated it, and it occurred,' implying two distinguishable aspects. Thus, the division of necessitation into being the same as the effect or being other is a false dichotomy because one of the two options (identity) is necessarily false by intuition. Next, the author presents an attempted answer: the cause necessitates in the first moment, and the effect occurs in the second moment, so there is no effect at the moment of necessitation and no necessitation at the moment of the effect's occurrence. This temporal separation seems to avoid the regress by making the necessitation and the effect temporally non-overlapping. However, an objection is raised against this answer: necessitation itself is a temporally originated event (أمر متجدد) and therefore requires a cause for its own attribution (اتصاف), which would be another necessitation, leading to an infinite regress. The objection is that the temporal separation answer does not eliminate the regress, because the first necessitation is still an event that needs a cause. The author then mentions that the better approach (الأولى) is to be discussed next.

تسلسل
علة
معلول
ضرورة
مغايرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The causal act of necessitation is identical to the occurrence of the effect.
Correction: Intuitive necessity shows they are distinguishable, as we say 'the cause necessitated it and it occurred'.

Checkpoint

C) The effect becomes identical to the cause

Key Takeaway
Positing a distinct causal act leads to infinite regress, and identifying it with the effect is impossible by intuition.

The Temporal Separation Answer and Its Objection
Presents the temporal separation answer and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the temporal separation answer to the regress objection and the counter-objection that it fails because the first necessitation itself requires a cause.

Core Idea
Separating cause and effect in time does not escape the regress of causes.

The author now considers a possible solution: the cause necessitates in the present and the effect occurs later, so they do not coexist. However, an objection is raised: this first necessitation is itself a new event that needs a cause, regenerating the regress. The passage ends by hinting at a better approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the temporal separation answer
Read the answer carefully: the cause necessitates in the first moment, and the effect exists in the second moment, so there is no overlap.
Note the key claim: no effect at the time of necessitation, and no necessitation at the time of effect.
2
Understand the objection
Read the objection: necessitation is a temporally originated event and therefore requires a cause for its own attribution, leading to another necessitation and regress.
The objection shows that temporal separation does not eliminate the need for a cause of the first act.
3
Reflect on the dialectic
Consider how the author sets up the 'better way' to follow, indicating that the temporal answer is insufficient.
This passage is part of a larger discussion; the solution is not yet presented.

إيجاب آخر، (بل) يكون ( إيجاباً) مغايراً لحصول المعلول، ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن كذلك، بل كان الإيجاب موجباً ( لزم التسلسل ) في الإيجاب ( مطلقاً)، وسواء كان الإيجاب حال وجود المعلول أو قبله، وسواء كان مغايراً لحصول المعلول أو لم يكن ) ولأن الضرورة تنفي كون الإيجاب (نفس) حصول ( المعلول) إذ كل أحد يعلم صدق قولنا : أوجبه العلة فحصل فترديد الإيجاب بين أن يكون نفسه أو غيره ترديد بين أمرين أحدهما لازم الانتفاء، وهو مستدرك مستقبح جداً. ( وقد يجاب بأنه ) إذا كانت العلة توجب في الحال وجود المعلول في ثاني الحال، فحينئذ ( لا معلول حال إيجاب العلة، وبالعكس ) أي لا إيجاب حال حصول المعلول فليس حصوله لإيجابها له، ولما أمكن أن يتطرق إليه المنع المذكور أولاً ، قال المصنف : ( والأولى ) في دفع
قوله : ( لأنه ليس موجباً قيل : إن الإيجاب أمر متجدد، فلا بد من علة الاتصاف ويتحقق إيجاب آخر ويلزم التسلسل البتة فتدبر .
Another necessitation (إيجاب), but it would be a necessitation different from the occurrence of the effect, and otherwise, i.e., if it were not so, but rather the necessitation were a necessitator, then an infinite regress (تسلسل) would necessarily follow in the necessitation absolutely, whether the necessitation is at the time of the effect's existence or before it, and whether it is different from the occurrence of the effect or not. And because intuitive necessity (الضرورة) negates that the necessitation is the very occurrence of the effect, since everyone knows the truth of our statement: 'The cause necessitated it, and it occurred.' So the division of necessitation between being itself or other is a division between two things, one of which is necessarily non-existent, and it is a preposterous and very ugly fallacy. And it may be answered that when the cause necessitates in the present the existence of the effect in the second moment, then there is no effect at the time of the cause's necessitation, and conversely, i.e., no necessitation at the time of the effect's occurrence, so its occurrence is not due to its necessitation of it. And since the aforementioned objection could potentially be directed against it, the author said: 'And the better (الأولى) way to repel...' His saying: 'Because it is not a necessitator.' It was said: 'Necessitation is a temporally originated matter, so there must be a cause of the attribution, and another necessitation occurs, and regress necessarily follows; so reflect.'
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument in the problem of the relationship between a cause's act of necessitation (إيجاب) and the effect's existence. The author begins by stating that if necessitation is considered distinct (مغاير) from the occurrence of the effect, then an infinite regress (تسلسل) would necessarily follow, regardless of whether the necessitation is simultaneous with or prior to the effect, and whether it is distinct or not. This is because each distinct necessitation would itself require a cause to bring it about, leading to an endless chain. The author then notes that intuitive necessity (الضرورة) rejects the idea that necessitation is identical to the effect, since we naturally say, 'The cause necessitated it, and it occurred,' implying two distinguishable aspects. Thus, the division of necessitation into being the same as the effect or being other is a false dichotomy because one of the two options (identity) is necessarily false by intuition. Next, the author presents an attempted answer: the cause necessitates in the first moment, and the effect occurs in the second moment, so there is no effect at the moment of necessitation and no necessitation at the moment of the effect's occurrence. This temporal separation seems to avoid the regress by making the necessitation and the effect temporally non-overlapping. However, an objection is raised against this answer: necessitation itself is a temporally originated event (أمر متجدد) and therefore requires a cause for its own attribution (اتصاف), which would be another necessitation, leading to an infinite regress. The objection is that the temporal separation answer does not eliminate the regress, because the first necessitation is still an event that needs a cause. The author then mentions that the better approach (الأولى) is to be discussed next.

علة الاتصاف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal separation solves the regress problem by making cause and effect non-simultaneous.
Correction: It does not because the first necessitation itself is an event requiring a cause, leading to regress.

Checkpoint

A) Because the effect cannot exist after the cause ceases
B) Because the first necessitation itself requires a cause
C) Because the cause cannot necessitate in the present for a future effect
D) Because the effect's occurrence is identical to the necessitation

Key Takeaway
Temporal separation does not resolve the infinite regress, as the first causal act itself demands a cause.

?
